| F-04 Denying a Conjunct                             | This fallacy draws too much from the denial of a conjunction. From “not both A and B,” it concludes one specific alternative without evidence. Knowing that two things are not both true does not tell us which one is false.                                                                                                                                                                            | Conjunction-negation error. **Form:** Not both A and B. Not A. Or: Not both A and B. Therefore, not B.                                                                      | “It cannot be true that the project is both cheap and high-quality. Therefore, it is not high-quality.” The real situation may be that it is high-quality but not cheap.                                                                           | If one conjunct is independently proven true, then the other must be false. Without that extra premise, the inference is incomplete.                                                      |
| F-05 Conjunction Fallacy                            | This fallacy treats a more detailed combined claim as more probable than one of its components. But “A and B” cannot be more probable than “A” alone, because every case of A and B is already a case of A.                                                                                                                                                                                              | Probability-form error. **Form:** P(A and B) is judged greater than P(A).                                                                                                   | “Mei is politically active and cares about climate issues. It is more likely that she is an environmental lawyer than that she is a lawyer.” The more specific claim may sound more vivid, but it is logically less or equally probable.           | Details can make a story feel coherent, but coherence is not probability. Extra conditions reduce or preserve probability; they do not increase it beyond the broader category.           |
| F-06 Disjunction Fallacy                            | This fallacy mishandles a set of alternatives by treating disjunction itself as if it gives stronger support than it does, or by failing to respect how alternatives are related. It often appears when someone treats “A or B” as established though neither A nor B has been sufficiently supported.                                                                                                   | Disjunction-support error. **Form:** Maybe A; maybe B; therefore A or B is treated as proven, and alternatives outside A/B are ignored.                                     | “Either the sales decline is caused by bad advertising or by lazy staff. Therefore, we only need to decide which one.” The argument excludes pricing, product quality, market conditions, and distribution problems without argument.              | If the alternatives are exhaustive and mutually exclusive, disjunctive reasoning can be strong. The fallacy lies in presenting an incomplete menu as if it were complete.                 |
| F-07 Commutation of Conditionals | This fallacy reverses a conditional. It treats “if P then Q” as equivalent to “if Q then P.” The error is assuming that a relation runs both ways merely because it runs one way. | Conditional reversal. **Form:** If P, then Q. Therefore, if Q, then P. | If a person is a judge, then the person has legal training. Therefore, if a person has legal training, the person is a judge. The reversed conditional is false because many lawyers, professors, and officials have legal training. *Case from the outline: Someone claims that developed countries often have strong football programs. A critic answers by naming a strong football country that is not developed, as if that refuted the original one-way conditional. The reply treats the conditional as if it were biconditional.* | Some conditionals are biconditionals, but that must be shown. “If and only if” supports reversal; ordinary “if” does not. |
| F-08 Illicit Contraposition                         | This fallacy forms an invalid contrapositive-like inference, especially when the original proposition does not permit the transformation. It often appears in categorical reasoning when terms are negated or reversed without preserving logical equivalence.                                                                                                                                           | Invalid contraposition. **Rough form:** All A are B. Therefore, all non-A are non-B.                                                                                        | “All cats are animals. Therefore, all non-cats are non-animals.” This is false: dogs are non-cats but still animals.                                                                                                                               | Valid contraposition works for conditionals: “If P then Q” implies “if not-Q then not-P.” It does not license every reversal or negation in ordinary categorical statements.              |
| F-09 Negating Antecedent and Consequent             | This fallacy resembles a failed transposition. The valid contrapositive of “if P then Q” is “if not-Q then not-P.” The fallacy negates both terms without switching them. The error is failing to transpose the antecedent and consequent when negating both.                                                                                                                 | Improper transposition. **Form:** If P, then Q. Therefore, if not-P, then not-Q.                                                                                            | If a person is a doctor, then they had medical training. Therefore, if a person is not a doctor, they had no medical training. This ignores nurses, researchers, former doctors, or medical graduates in other jobs.                               | The correct contrapositive is: if someone had no medical training, then they are not a doctor. Negating both sides without reversing them is the error.                                   |
| F-10 Affirmative Conclusion from a Negative Premise | In categorical syllogisms, a negative premise indicates exclusion between classes. If one premise is negative, the conclusion must also be negative. This fallacy draws an affirmative conclusion from a negative premise.                                                                                                                                                                               | Syllogistic-form error. **Form:** No A are B. Some C are A. Therefore, some C are B.                                                                                        | No reptiles are mammals. Some pets are reptiles. Therefore, some pets are mammals. The premises exclude reptiles from mammals; they cannot establish affirmative inclusion.                                                                        | If another independent affirmative premise connects C to B, an affirmative conclusion may be possible. But it cannot be drawn from the negative relation alone.                           |
| F-11 Negative Conclusion from Affirmative Premises  | If both premises in a categorical syllogism are affirmative, they establish inclusion or overlap, not exclusion. A negative conclusion requires some negative relation in the premises.                                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Syllogistic-form error. **Form:** All A are B. All C are A. Therefore, no C are B.                                                                                          | All poets are language users. All haiku writers are poets. Therefore, no haiku writers are language users. The conclusion contradicts the inclusive direction of the premises.                                                                     | A negative conclusion can be valid if a negative premise is supplied. Without it, exclusion has not been established.                                                                     |
| F-12 Exclusive Premises                             | This fallacy occurs when a syllogism has two negative premises. Two exclusions do not establish a positive relation between the remaining terms. If both premises say what things are not, no valid connection is made between subject and predicate.                                                                                                                                                    | Syllogistic-form error. **Form:** No A are B. No C are B. Therefore, C are A.                                                                                               | No cats are reptiles. No dogs are reptiles. Therefore, dogs are cats. The two classes are both excluded from reptiles, but that does not connect them to each other.                                                                               | Negative information can be useful in elimination reasoning, but a valid categorical conclusion needs a proper connection among the terms.                                                |
| F-13 Existential Fallacy                            | This fallacy draws an existential conclusion from universal premises that do not themselves assert existence. In modern logic, “all unicorns are horned” does not imply that unicorns exist.                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Quantifier/existence error. **Form:** All A are B. Therefore, some A exist.                                                                                                 | All golden mountains are mountains. Therefore, some golden mountains exist. The universal statement can be vacuously true even if the class has no members.                                                                                        | In traditional Aristotelian logic, universal statements were often treated as existentially loaded. In modern formal logic, existence must be stated or otherwise established.            |
| F-14 False Conversion                               | This fallacy reverses a categorical statement when reversal is not logically permitted. “All A are B” does not imply “all B are A.”                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      | Categorical conversion error. **Form:** All A are B. Therefore, all B are A.                                                                                                | All surgeons are doctors. Therefore, all doctors are surgeons. The original relation is one-way class inclusion.                                                                                                                                   | Some propositions convert validly, such as “No A are B” implies “No B are A.” “Some A are B” also converts to “Some B are A.” Universal affirmative statements do not convert simply.     |
| F-15 Statement of Conversion                        | This is a related conversion error in which a speaker treats a converted statement as if it preserved the meaning of the original. The problem is the same: class inclusion is reversed without justification.                                                                                                                                                                                           | Conversion error. **Form:** A statement S is converted into S′, and S′ is treated as equivalent to S when it is not.                                                        | “Every expert on aviation law understands airport regulation” becomes “everyone who understands airport regulation is an expert on aviation law.” The converted statement is stronger and false.                                                   | Conversion is legitimate only for forms that preserve truth under conversion. The arguer must check the type of categorical proposition before reversing it.                              |
| F-16 Fallacy of Opposition                          | This fallacy misuses the logical opposition between propositions, especially by treating contraries, contradictories, subcontraries, or subaltern relations as if they worked the same way. The error lies in mishandling what follows from the truth or falsity of a related proposition.                                                                                                               | Opposition-square error. **Rough form:** Proposition A is false; therefore, a related but not contradictory proposition B is true.                                          | “It is false that all students passed. Therefore, no students passed.” The negation of “all passed” is “not all passed,” not “none passed.”                                                                                                        | If the two propositions are true contradictories, the inference can work. The problem appears when a contrary or weaker opposite is treated as a strict contradictory.                    |
| F-17 `Fallacy of Four Terms`                        | A categorical syllogism should have three terms: major, minor, and middle. This fallacy introduces a fourth term, often by using the same word in two different senses. The argument appears syllogistic, but the middle connection fails.                                                                                                                                                               | Syllogistic/semantic-form error. **Form:** All A are B. All C are D. Therefore, C are B, where B and D are treated as the same but are not.                                 | Nothing is better than justice. A sandwich is better than nothing. Therefore, a sandwich is better than justice. “Nothing” has shifted meaning, creating a hidden fourth term.                                                                     | If the apparently different terms are explicitly shown to be equivalent in the same sense, the problem disappears. Otherwise the syllogism does not have a real middle term.              |
| F-18 `Fallacy of the Undistributed Middle`          | The middle term must connect the major and minor terms. If it is not distributed in either premise, the two outer terms may merely share membership in a broad class without being connected to each other. The form “All Catholics are Christian; all Christians are Jesus lovers; therefore all Jesus lovers are Catholic” shows how the relevant class relation is overextended.  | Syllogistic distribution error. **Form:** All A are C. All B are C. Therefore, all A are B.                                                                                 | All cats are animals. All dogs are animals. Therefore, all cats are dogs. “Animals” is too broad to connect cats and dogs.                                                                                                                         | If the middle term is properly distributed, the syllogism may become valid. Sharing a predicate is not enough; the middle term must actually link the classes.                            |
| F-19 Illicit Major                                  | This fallacy occurs when the major term is distributed in the conclusion but not distributed in the major premise. The conclusion says more about the major term than the premises allow.                                                                                                                                                                                                                | Syllogistic distribution error. **Form:** All A are B. No C are A. Therefore, no C are B.                                                                                   | All poets are writers. No accountants are poets. Therefore, no accountants are writers. The conclusion excludes accountants from all writers, but the premise only excluded them from poets.                                                       | If the major premise distributes the major term appropriately, the inference may be valid. The issue is overextending the conclusion beyond the distribution in the premises.             |
| F-20 Illicit Minor                                  | This fallacy occurs when the minor term is distributed in the conclusion but not in the minor premise. The conclusion makes a universal claim about the minor term that the premises did not support.                                                                                                                                                                                                    | Syllogistic distribution error. **Form:** All A are B. All A are C. Therefore, all C are B.                                                                                 | All violinists are musicians. All violinists are disciplined. Therefore, all disciplined people are musicians. The conclusion distributes “disciplined people” too broadly.                                                                        | A valid syllogism must distribute in the premises any term distributed in the conclusion. Otherwise the conclusion overclaims.                                                            |
| F-21 Fallacy of Every and All                       | This fallacy confuses distributive and collective readings of “every” and “all.” What is true of each member separately may not be true of the group collectively, and what is true of the group may not be true of each member.                                                                                                                                                                         | Quantifier/distribution error. **Form:** Every member has property P. Therefore, the whole group has P in the same way. Or: the group has P; therefore, every member has P. | Every player on the team is individually talented. Therefore, the team is tactically excellent. Individual talent does not automatically create collective coordination.                                                                           | Some properties distribute cleanly: if every tile is red, the floor is red. The question is whether the predicate is distributive, collective, relational, or emergent.                   |
| F-22 Quantifier-Shift Fallacy                       | This fallacy shifts the scope of quantifiers. It moves from “for every person, there exists something” to “there exists one thing for every person,” or the reverse.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     | Quantifier-scope error. **Form:** For every X, there is some Y. Therefore, there is one Y for every X.                                                                      | Everyone has a favorite book. Therefore, there is one book that everyone has as their favorite. The first claim allows different books for different people.                                                                                       | If the premises actually establish the same object for all cases, the conclusion can follow. The fallacy is a scope shift, not the mere use of quantifiers.                               |
| F-23 Modal Scope Fallacy                            | This fallacy confuses what is necessarily true with what is true of necessity in a particular arrangement. It shifts the scope of necessity or possibility.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Modal-scope error. **Form:** Necessarily, if P then Q. Therefore, if P, then necessarily Q.                                                                                 | Necessarily, if someone is the winner, they received the most votes. Anna is the winner. Therefore, Anna necessarily received the most votes. She did receive the most votes, but she did not have to be the winner in all possible circumstances. | Modal reasoning is valid when the scope of necessity is preserved. The error is moving necessity from the conditional relation to the particular consequent.                              |
| F-24 `Non Sequitur`                                   | `Non sequitur` means “it does not follow.” It is a broad label for an argument whose conclusion is not supported by its premises. It may name a formal invalidity, but in ordinary use it often marks a gap too large to classify more specifically.                                                                                                                                                     | General inference failure. **Form:** P. Therefore, Q, where P gives no adequate support for Q.                                                                              | “This professor dresses casually; therefore, his theory of contract law is wrong.” The premise does not support the conclusion.                                                                                                                    | Because the term is broad, it should not be used lazily. If the specific error is identifiable, name the more precise fallacy.                                                            |
| F-25 `Inconsistency`                                  | Inconsistency occurs when a set of claims cannot all be true together. The error is not merely changing one’s mind, but maintaining incompatible commitments at the same time.                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Contradiction/commitment error. **Form:** P. Not P. Or: P entails Q, but speaker also denies Q.                                                                             | “All speech should be completely unrestricted. Also, speech that offends me should be legally banned.” The two commitments conflict unless a principled distinction is supplied.                                                                   | Apparent inconsistency may disappear if terms are clarified or contexts differ. A person may hold different rules for different settings if the difference is justified.                  |
| F-26 Conflicting Conditions                         | This fallacy sets conditions that cannot be jointly satisfied, then criticizes the opponent for failing to meet them. It is a structural trap rather than a simple false premise.                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Incompatible-requirement error. **Form:** To be acceptable, X must satisfy A and not-A. X cannot do both. Therefore, X is rejected.                                         | “A good theory must be simple enough for beginners and at the same time cover every technical exception in full detail.” The standard demands mutually conflicting features.                                                                       | Some trade-offs are real and can be negotiated. The fallacy appears when incompatible conditions are imposed as if they were jointly reasonable requirements.                             |
| F-27 Illicit Substitution of Identicals             | This fallacy substitutes two extensionally identical terms inside an intensional context such as “believes,” “knows,” “wants,” or “imagines.” Cases like Superman / Clark Kent show the point: two names can refer to the same person, but someone may know or believe something under one description and not the other.                                                                    | Intensional-context error. **Form:** X is Y. Person believes something about X. Therefore, person believes it about Y.                                                      | Lois believes Superman can fly. Superman is Clark Kent. Therefore, Lois believes Clark Kent can fly. The substitution fails because her belief is tied to one description, not the other.                                                          | In purely extensional contexts, substitution of identicals is usually valid. The problem appears in contexts involving belief, knowledge, desire, doubt, necessity, or description.       |
| F-28 Denying the Correlative                        | This fallacy rejects a genuine binary structure by introducing a third option where the terms are logically exhaustive. It is the opposite problem of false dilemma: here the dilemma really is exhaustive, but the speaker evades it.                                                                                                                                                                   | Correlative-choice error. **Form:** Either X or not-X. Therefore, Y is offered as if it avoids the choice.                                                                  | “Did you submit the assignment or not?” “I worked very hard on it.” The answer may be relevant emotionally, but it does not answer the exhaustive question.                                                                                        | Many real-world choices are not exhaustive, so refusing a false binary can be reasonable. The fallacy occurs only when the alternatives are genuinely exhaustive in the relevant respect. |
| F-29 Suppressed Correlative                         | This fallacy redefines one side of a contrast so broadly or narrowly that the opposing category becomes impossible or meaningless. A correlative pair works only if both sides remain available.                                                                                                                                                                                                         | Correlative-definition error. **Form:** X is defined so that non-X cannot meaningfully exist. Therefore, all cases are treated as X.                                        | “Anyone who disagrees with reason is irrational; anyone who disagrees with my view disagrees with reason; therefore, no rational disagreement with my view exists.” The definition suppresses the opposing category.                               | Strong definitions are allowed when justified. The fallacy appears when the definition is engineered to prevent possible counterexamples or dissent.                                      |

## Premise, Presupposition, Circularity, and Question-Framing Errors

These fallacies belong together because the argument is defective before genuine inference can begin. The conclusion may already be hidden inside the premise; the question may force the respondent to accept an unwelcome presupposition; the definition may be arranged so that counterexamples cannot appear; or the range of possible answers may be artificially restricted. Questions require contextual judgment: not every complex question is fallacious, and even loaded questions become defective only when presuppositions are used coercively, so that the respondent is pushed into commitments he has not accepted. `Begging the question`, `circular reasoning`, `complex question`, `false dilemma`, and related items are distinct but connected errors in how premises, definitions, and starting assumptions are handled.

| Fallacy           | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Mechanism                                                                                                            | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                                                                |
| ------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| P-01 `Begging the Question` | This fallacy assumes what it needs to prove. The argument appears to move from premise to conclusion, but the premise already contains the disputed conclusion in a disguised form. The error is not merely repetition; it is using a contested claim as if it were already granted. | Premise-contamination error. **Form:** X is true because Y; but Y is true only if X is already accepted. | “This doctrine is reliable because the sacred text says so; the sacred text is reliable because the doctrine says it is divinely protected.” The argument never leaves the disputed circle. | All reasoning depends on some starting points. It becomes fallacious when the exact point under dispute is smuggled into the premise instead of being independently supported. |
| P-02 `Circular Reasoning` | Circular reasoning is close to begging the question, but the circular structure is often more explicit. Claim A is supported by claim B, while B is supported by A. A religious text validating itself through its own authority is a standard case of this pattern. | Circular-support error. **Form:** A because B; B because A. | “The policy is legitimate because the committee approved it; the committee’s approval is legitimate because this policy authorized the committee.” The authority of each side depends on the other. | Some philosophical systems return to first principles, but a large justificatory circle is not automatically the same as a small vicious circle. The question is whether the argument gives independent support or only repeats itself. |
| P-03 Circular Definition | A circular definition defines a term by using the same term, a synonym, or a concept that depends on the original term. It fails because the definition does not clarify meaning; it merely moves the reader around the same concept. | Definition-loop error. **Form:** X means Y; Y means X. | “Justice is what is just.” Or: “A rational belief is a belief held rationally.” The definition gives no usable criterion. | Some technical definitions are interdependent inside a theory. They are acceptable only if the whole system eventually connects to clearer primitives, examples, rules, or operational criteria. |
| P-04 `Complex Question Fallacy` | A complex question combines several issues into one question in a way that makes separate answers difficult. It becomes fallacious when the respondent is forced to answer as if all parts must receive the same answer or as if all presuppositions are accepted. Complex questions are not inherently fallacious; many technical or practical dialogues require them. | Question-framing error. **Form:** Question Q contains A + B + C, but the respondent may accept A and reject B or C. | “Do you support fair hiring practices and mandatory quotas?” A respondent may support fair hiring and oppose quotas, but the yes/no form compresses two issues into one answer. | A complex question is acceptable when its parts naturally belong together or when the respondent can divide the answer. The fallacy appears when division is blocked or when the complexity is used aggressively. |
| P-05 `Loaded Question` | A loaded question contains a presupposition that the respondent has not accepted and would not want to accept. The classic form is designed so that either direct answer seems to concede the damaging presupposition. Loaded questions contain “unwelcome commitments” relative to the answerer’s position. | Presupposition trap. **Form:** Answering Q requires accepting hidden claim Y. | “Have you stopped falsifying your data?” A “yes” suggests past falsification; a “no” suggests continuing falsification. The proper response is to reject the presupposition. *Case from the outline: A person asks, “You also think this plan is obviously smarter, right?” The question pretends to seek an opinion, but it pressures the listener to accept the preferred answer as already evident.* | Not every presupposition is problematic. “Did you pick up your coat?” presupposes there is a coat, but this may be harmless. A loaded question becomes fallacious when the presupposition is controversial, damaging, and unaccepted. |
| P-06 Many Questions Fallacy | This fallacy packages several questions into one, often to make a simple answer misleading. It overlaps with complex question, but the emphasis is on multiplicity: the respondent is made to answer several distinct issues as if they were one issue. | Multiple-question compression. **Form:** Q1 + Q2 + Q3 are asked as one question, requiring one answer. | “Did the manager ignore the warning, conceal the report, and mislead the board?” These are three claims. One may be true while another is false. | If the question is explicitly exploratory and the answerer is allowed to separate the parts, it may be legitimate. The fallacy lies in forcing a bundled answer. |
| P-07 `False Dilemma` / Black-or-White Fallacy | This fallacy presents two options as if they exhaust all possibilities when more options exist. It narrows the decision space and makes one preferred option appear necessary by eliminating alternatives in advance. | Artificial-choice error. **Form:** Either A or B. Not A. Therefore B. But C, D, or mixed positions are possible. | “Either you support this surveillance bill, or you do not care about public safety.” One may oppose the bill while supporting other safety measures. *Case from the outline: A debate about a public figure is reduced to two labels: either you praise everything and count as a fan, or you criticize one point and count as a hater. Mixed judgment is removed before evidence is considered.* | Some choices really are binary: submit or do not submit; guilty or not guilty under a specific charge. The fallacy occurs when a non-binary issue is forced into a binary frame. |
| P-08 Alleged Certainty | This fallacy presents a claim as certain when the evidence supports only probability, suspicion, tendency, or possibility. It protects the claim by rhetorically eliminating uncertainty before the reasoning has earned that elimination. | Overcertainty / epistemic inflation. **Form:** Evidence weakly supports X; speaker presents X as unquestionable. | “The timing of the resignation proves there was corruption.” Timing may raise suspicion, but it does not by itself prove corruption. *Case from the outline: After hearing only a few scattered details, a speaker declares his interpretation “the correct and rational one” and calls it sharp and final. Certainty is performed before the support reaches that level.* | Strong evidence can justify high confidence. The fallacy lies in using the language of certainty where the evidence only warrants a weaker conclusion. |
| P-09 Appeal to Self-evident Truth | This fallacy declares a contested proposition “self-evident” in order to avoid defending it. It treats the need for argument as a sign of irrationality in the opponent, rather than showing why the proposition really requires no proof. | Assertion-as-foundation error. **Form:** X is obviously true; therefore no argument is needed. | “It is self-evident that only one economic system can produce freedom.” That is a substantive political and historical claim, not a self-evident axiom. | Some propositions may function as basic assumptions in a system. But in public argument, if reasonable participants dispute a claim, simply calling it self-evident does not settle it. |
| P-10 Definition as Premise-Frame | This is the premise-framing variant of appeal to definition. A definition is placed among the starting assumptions so that a substantive dispute looks settled before argument begins. | Definition-as-starting-point error. **Form:** Word W is defined as X; therefore the contested issue about W is treated as already fixed. | “Marriage means union between a man and a woman; therefore same-sex marriage is impossible.” The definition is being used to close an institutional and normative dispute at the starting line. | The fuller language error is listed under L-12. Here the focus is narrower: a definition is being used as an unargued premise. |
| P-11 Evaluative Definition as Premise-Frame | This is the premise-framing variant of the definist fallacy. An evaluative definition is inserted at the beginning of the argument, so the opponent loses by accepting the vocabulary. | Loaded-definition premise error. **Form:** Define X with evaluative content E; then use E as if it had been independently established. | “Real education is liberation from institutions; therefore schools do not provide real education.” The anti-institutional evaluation has been placed inside the definition. | The fuller semantic version is listed under L-10. Here the issue is its use as a starting premise. |
| P-12 Failure to Elucidate | This fallacy occurs when a speaker relies on vague or technical terms but refuses to clarify them sufficiently for the argument to be evaluated. The claim remains protected because no one can tell what would count as support or refutation. | Clarification failure. **Form:** X is asserted using unclear key term T; requests for clarification are not answered. | “The policy violates authentic social harmony.” If “authentic social harmony” is never clarified, the claim cannot be assessed. | Not every term can be fully defined at once. The fallacy appears when clarification is necessary for evaluation and the speaker avoids it. |
| P-13 Meaningless Question | A meaningless question has no determinate answer because its terms, assumptions, or categories fail to form a coherent inquiry. It may sound profound, but the form of the question prevents meaningful resolution. | Pseudo-question error. **Form:** Question Q uses unclear or incoherent terms, so no answer can be evaluated. | “What color is justice on a Tuesday?” Unless given metaphorical rules, the question combines categories without a coherent standard of answer. | Some unusual questions are meaningful in poetry, theology, philosophy, or fiction if the context supplies interpretive rules. The fallacy lies in presenting incoherence as ordinary argument. |
| P-14 `Accident Fallacy` | The accident fallacy applies a general rule to a case where relevant exceptions or special circumstances defeat that application. The rule may be sound in ordinary cases, but the arguer treats it as exceptionless. | Misapplied general rule. **Form:** Generally, if A then B. Case C is A. Therefore B, ignoring relevant exception E. | “People should not break windows. Firefighters broke the window to rescue a child. Therefore, the firefighters acted wrongly.” The emergency changes the application of the rule. | General rules are necessary. The fallacy appears when relevant circumstances are ignored; not every appeal to exception is legitimate. |
| P-15 Overwhelming Exception | This is the opposite danger: a rule is stated with so many exceptions that the rule loses its force. The exception overwhelms the generalization, making the original claim misleading or empty. | Rule-eroded-by-exceptions error. **Form:** X is generally true, except in most important cases. | “This investment is safe, except during inflation, recession, market volatility, interest-rate changes, or geopolitical risk.” The exceptions cover the situations where safety most matters. | Some rules genuinely need qualifications. The fallacy appears when the qualifications make the rule practically useless while the speaker continues to present it as strong. |
| P-16 Ad Hoc Rescue | An ad hoc rescue adds a new assumption only after a claim is challenged, solely to protect the original claim. The added premise has no independent support and functions as a patch. | Protective-hypothesis error. **Form:** Claim X is challenged by evidence E; add unsupported Y so X survives. | “My prediction failed because invisible energy fields changed the result at the last moment.” The rescue explains away failure without independent evidence. | Revising a theory in light of evidence is legitimate. It becomes ad hoc when the revision is unsupported, unfalsifiable, and introduced only to avoid defeat. |
| P-17 Just Because Fallacy | This fallacy treats a bare assertion as a reason. It often appears when the speaker offers “just because” as if refusal to explain were itself a justification. | Reason-substitution error. **Form:** X is true/right because it just is. | “Why should this group get less protection?” “Because that is just how society works.” The answer restates acceptance, not justification. | Some conversations end with practical authority or convention, but a demand for reasons cannot be answered by mere insistence if the issue remains disputed. |
| P-18 Just In Case Fallacy | This fallacy treats a remote possibility as a sufficient reason for action without considering probability, cost, trade-offs, or alternative risks. It often turns precaution into unlimited justification. | Possibility-inflation error. **Form:** X might happen; therefore we must act as if X is likely or decisive. | “There is a tiny chance this harmless-looking book might influence someone badly; therefore it should be banned.” Possibility alone is doing too much work. | Precaution is reasonable when risk is severe, probability is non-negligible, and cost is proportionate. The fallacy appears when possibility alone overrides all other considerations. |
| P-19 Having Your Cake | This fallacy tries to keep two incompatible advantages or positions at once. The speaker wants the benefit of a claim while refusing the burden or implication that comes with it. | Incompatible-commitment error. **Form:** Claim X is used when beneficial; its consequences are denied when costly. | “Our organization is powerful enough to deserve special influence, but too powerless to be held responsible for outcomes.” The speaker wants authority without accountability. | Complex institutions can have power in one respect and weakness in another. The fallacy appears when the same respect is shifted without acknowledgment. |
| P-20 Kettle Logic | Kettle logic offers several defenses that contradict each other, hoping that the accumulation of excuses will persuade even though they cannot all be true. The name comes from the pattern: “I returned the kettle undamaged; it was already damaged; I never borrowed it.” | Inconsistent-defense pileup. **Form:** Defense A, defense B, and defense C are each offered, but they cannot all be true. | “I did not leak the document; the document was already public; and leaking it was justified.” Some of these may be individually arguable, but together they clash. | Alternative arguments can be legitimate when explicitly presented as hypotheticals: “Even if A fails, B may apply.” It becomes kettle logic when incompatible defenses are asserted together as true. |
| P-21 Stolen Concept Fallacy | This fallacy relies on a concept while denying the conditions that make the concept possible. The argument undercuts its own conceptual foundation. | Conceptual-dependence error. **Form:** Use concept C while denying prerequisite P for C. | “There is no such thing as meaning; and by that sentence I mean exactly what it says.” The statement depends on meaning while denying meaning. | Accusations of stolen concept can be overused. The critic must show a real dependency between the denied condition and the concept being used. |
| P-22 Counterfactual Premise Overreach | This is the premise-framing variant of hypothesis contrary to fact. A speculative counterfactual is placed into the premises as if it were a stable fact, and a strong conclusion is then drawn from it. | Counterfactual-premise error. **Form:** If unreal X had happened, Y would certainly have happened; therefore strong conclusion Z follows. | “If that leader had never taken office, the economy would definitely have boomed.” The counterfactual premise carries more certainty than the evidence can support. | The comparison and thought-experiment version is listed under M-21. Here the problem is treating the counterfactual as a settled premise. |
| P-23 Far-Fetched Hypothesis | This fallacy protects a claim by proposing an extremely unlikely explanation when simpler and better-supported explanations exist. It is not merely a creative hypothesis; it is an implausible rescue offered without adequate reason. | Implausible-explanation error. **Form:** Evidence E threatens X; propose remote H to preserve X. | “The exam scores dropped because a rare electromagnetic disturbance affected only our students’ memory.” Ordinary explanations such as poor preparation are ignored. | Unusual explanations can be true. The problem is choosing the least plausible explanation without evidence, especially to avoid a more likely conclusion. |
| P-24 Least Plausible Hypothesis | This is a stronger form of the previous error: among available explanations, the speaker selects the least plausible one as if it were preferred. The defect lies in reversing explanatory discipline. | Bad-explanation preference. **Form:** H1, H2, H3 explain E; H3 is least plausible; speaker chooses H3 without reason. | A politician’s account has contradictions. Instead of considering deception, confusion, or poor record-keeping, a supporter claims “time-zone-induced memory distortion” explains it all. | If the least expected hypothesis has strong independent evidence, it may be accepted. Implausibility is not impossibility; the fallacy lies in unsupported preference. |
| P-25 Fantasy Projection | Fantasy projection treats an imagined possibility as if it were reality. The speaker fills missing evidence with a desired or feared story, then reasons from that story as though it were established. | Imagination-as-premise error. **Form:** I can imagine X; therefore X is treated as actual or likely. | “The company might be secretly controlled by foreign agents; therefore every management decision should be interpreted as sabotage.” The imagined story governs interpretation before evidence appears. | Hypotheses can begin imaginatively. They become fallacious when imagination substitutes for evidence and starts controlling the conclusion. |
| P-26 Appeal to Possibility | This fallacy treats mere possibility as if it were probability, evidence, or proof. “Could be” becomes “probably is” or “must be considered decisive.” | Possibility-to-proof error. **Form:** X is possible. Therefore X is likely/true/decisive. | “It is possible that the witness lied. Therefore, the whole case collapses.” Possibility alone does not establish actual lying. | Possibility can justify further investigation when stakes are high. But possibility must not be inflated into likelihood without evidence. |
| P-27 Appeal to Complexity | This fallacy uses complexity to avoid evaluation. Because an issue is complicated, the speaker implies that no criticism, conclusion, or practical judgment can be made. | Complexity-as-shield error. **Form:** X is complex. Therefore, no judgment about X is possible, or my position cannot be challenged. | “The financial system is too complex for outsiders to criticize bank conduct.” Complexity may require caution, but it does not eliminate accountability. | Some issues really require expertise and careful qualification. The fallacy lies in using complexity to block all scrutiny. |
| P-28 Confusing Currently Unexplained with Unexplainable | This fallacy turns a current gap in explanation into a permanent limit of explanation. It mistakes “we do not yet know” for “no one can know” or “science can never explain this.” | Ignorance-to-impossibility error. **Form:** X is not currently explained. Therefore X is unexplainable. | “Researchers do not yet fully understand this disease mechanism; therefore medicine will never explain it.” The conclusion outruns the evidence. | Some questions may indeed be beyond current methods, but that must be argued. Lack of present explanation is not proof of permanent inexplicability. |
| P-29 Imposter Fallacy | This fallacy rejects a person’s or thing’s membership in a category by imposing an unstable or unfair standard of authenticity. It overlaps with purity-based reasoning but often targets identity, competence, or legitimacy. | Authenticity-gatekeeping error. **Form:** X appears to belong to group G, but is dismissed as not “real” G without stable criteria. | “She publishes serious research, but she is not a real philosopher because she writes clearly for the public.” The standard is used to exclude rather than evaluate. | Categories need criteria. The fallacy appears when criteria are vague, shifting, or designed mainly to delegitimize. |
| P-30 Alternative Advance | This fallacy introduces a new alternative or criterion after the original line of argument is weakened, not to improve inquiry but to avoid concession. It is related to moving the goalposts, but its emphasis is on advancing a fresh route before admitting the old one failed. | Evasion-by-new-alternative error. **Form:** Defense A fails; instead of conceding, speaker advances B as if the burden never existed. | “The document proves my claim.” When the document is shown not to prove it: “Well, the real proof is in people’s lived experience.” The new route may matter, but the failure of the first route is not acknowledged. | Revising an argument is legitimate if the speaker admits the earlier argument failed and gives reasons for the new one. It becomes fallacious when the shift is used to avoid accountability. |

## Relevance and Topic-Diversion Errors

These fallacies belong together because they disturb the relation between **the issue that needs to be answered** and **the material actually offered as a response**. The arguer may attack a distorted version of the opponent’s position, prove a different conclusion, avoid the question, replace the issue with a dramatic side issue, or use a formally impressive but practically irrelevant objection. Relevance is often dialectical: to decide whether an argument is relevant, one must first determine what conclusion was supposed to be proved and what the original issue was.

| Fallacy                                | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Mechanism                                                                                                                                          | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| R-01 `Straw Man Fallacy` / Strawman | This fallacy misrepresents the opponent’s position so that it becomes easier to attack. The defect is not merely disagreement or simplification; it is a distortion of commitment. The opponent is made responsible for a claim that he did not assert, imply, or need to accept. A beer-advertising example shows this clearly: a citizen supports banning beer and wine advertising on radio and television, but the industry representative treats him as if he wants people to stop drinking altogether. That stronger claim is not part of the citizen’s actual commitment. | Commitment-distortion error. **Form:** A asserts X. B reconstructs X as stronger/weaker/more absurd Y. B attacks Y. B claims X has been refuted. | “We should regulate facial-recognition technology.” “So you want to ban all technological progress and send society back to the Stone Age.” The second speaker attacks a fabricated anti-technology position, not the regulation claim. | A charitable reconstruction is not a straw man if it is based on the opponent’s actual words, necessary assumptions, or accepted implications. Strong criticism should first reconstruct the opponent’s real position in a form the opponent could recognize. |
| R-02 `Red Herring` / Ignoratio Elenchi / Misdirection / Changing the Subject | A red herring diverts attention from the issue that needs to be answered. The new topic may be emotionally powerful, interesting, or even important, but it does not discharge the current burden of proof. Red herring introduces another issue in a way that abandons the original argument; it differs from wrong conclusion because its main mechanism is diversion and distraction. | Topic-diversion error. **Form:** Argument A is under discussion. Speaker introduces B. Attention moves to B. A is left unanswered. | “Why did you falsify the expense report?” “What is ‘honesty,’ really? Every society defines it differently.” The philosophical topic may be interesting, but it evades the concrete accusation. | Introducing background is legitimate if it helps answer the original issue. It becomes a red herring when the new material replaces rather than supports the needed answer. |
| R-03 `Irrelevant Conclusion` / Ignoratio Elenchi / Wrong Conclusion | This fallacy proves something other than the conclusion that was supposed to be proved. The argument may contain true premises and may even establish a real point, but the point is not the one at issue. The courtroom example is direct: a prosecutor argues that murder is horrible, when what must be proved is that the defendant committed murder. | Wrong-target proof. **Form:** Need to prove X. Argument proves Y. Y may be true, but Y does not establish X. | “This policy has administrative costs. Therefore, the policy should be abolished.” The argument has shown a cost, not that the cost outweighs benefits or that abolition is the best response. | A sub-conclusion can be relevant if it is explicitly part of a larger proof. The fallacy appears when a partial or different conclusion is presented as if it settled the main issue. |
| R-04 Avoiding the Issue / Avoiding the Question / Missing the Point | Avoiding the issue differs from red herring in degree and strategy. Red herring usually introduces a deliberate diversion; avoiding the issue may simply fail to address the argument. A typical example has one speaker ask whether religious belief would differ if one had been raised elsewhere; the reply shifts to “your faith is weak” rather than answering the question. | Non-response error. **Form:** A asks or argues X. B responds with Y, which does not address X. | “Did the report include the missing safety tests?” “Our team worked very hard under impossible deadlines.” Hard work does not answer whether the tests were included. | Sometimes a direct answer is unfair because the question is loaded. In that case, challenging the presupposition is not evasion. A reply criticized as evasive may be reasonable when the original question is coercive. |
| R-05 Logic Chopping / Quibbling / Nit-picking / Splitting Hairs | Logic chopping uses technical precision in a way that distracts from the practical issue. The problem is not careful reasoning itself, but disproportionate attention to trivial distinctions when the main question remains unanswered. It uses logical tools pedantically by focusing on trivial details instead of directly addressing the dispute. | Trivial-objection distraction. **Form:** Main claim X is made. Speaker attacks a minor wording detail Y. Discussion of X is derailed. | “Can you help move this broken car out of traffic?” “Before I answer, define ‘help,’ ‘move,’ and whether the car is really ‘broken’ in a metaphysical sense.” The precision blocks action on the relevant issue. *Case from the outline: Someone says sleep is like charging a phone battery. The listener objects that not every device slows down at low power. The objection may be technically true, but it dodges the intended point about rest and cognitive performance.* | Fine distinctions matter in law, philosophy, science, and technical writing. It becomes fallacious when precision is used to evade rather than clarify the main question. |
| R-06 `Tu Quoque` / Appeal to Hypocrisy / “You Too” | This fallacy answers criticism by accusing the critic of similar wrongdoing. It may expose inconsistency, but it does not answer the original charge. The defect is replacing “Is the criticism true?” with “Is the critic consistent?” | Counter-accusation as evasion. **Form:** A criticizes B for X. B replies that A also did X. Therefore B need not answer. | “Your article cherry-picks data.” “You cherry-picked examples last month.” Even if true, the reply does not show that the current article’s data selection is sound. | Hypocrisy may be relevant if the issue is the critic’s credibility, enforcement consistency, or sincerity. But it usually does not prove the original criticism false. |
| R-07 `Fallacy Fallacy` / Argument from Fallacy / Argumentum ad Logicam | This fallacy concludes that a claim is false because an argument for it contains a fallacy. The form is direct: argument X is fallacious; therefore the conclusion of X is false. The error is confusing failure of support with proof of the opposite. | Bad-argument-to-false-conclusion error. **Form:** Argument for X is fallacious. Therefore X is false. | “He defended the medicine with an anecdote, so the medicine must not work.” The anecdote does not establish effectiveness, but it also does not prove ineffectiveness. | If independent evidence shows X is false, one may reject X. But the mere fact that someone argued badly for X only shows that this argument fails. |
| R-08 Relative Privation / “Not as Bad as” / Appeal to Worse Problems | This fallacy dismisses a problem by pointing to a more serious problem. The error is treating “not the worst problem” as equivalent to “not a real problem.” It shifts the issue from whether X deserves attention to whether something worse exists. | Severity-displacement error. **Form:** X is a problem. Y is worse than X. Therefore X should not be discussed or addressed. | “The library has mold and broken heating.” “Some people do not even have libraries, so stop complaining.” The worse condition elsewhere does not make the local problem disappear. *Case from the outline: “I am exhausted and need rest.” “Some people get up at four in the morning to sell breakfast.” The harder case may deserve sympathy too, but it does not answer the present need for rest.* | If the discussion is about resource allocation or priority, comparing severity is legitimate. The fallacy appears when comparison is used to cancel a problem rather than rank it. |
| R-09 `Two Wrongs Make a Right` | This fallacy claims that one wrongful act is justified because another wrongful act has occurred. It is a relevance error because the other wrong may explain anger or retaliation, but it does not by itself justify the present act. | Retaliatory-justification error. **Form:** They did wrong X. Therefore we may do wrong Y. | “They spread false rumors about us, so we are justified in fabricating accusations against them.” The first wrong does not make the second truthful or justified. | Some responses to wrongdoing are justified: self-defense, legal punishment, restitution, or proportional sanction. The fallacy lies in treating any counter-wrong as automatically justified. |
| R-10 Appeal to Extremes | This fallacy turns a reasonable claim into an absurd extreme and then attacks that extreme. It differs from legitimate reductio ad absurdum because the extreme implication is manufactured rather than derived. | Extreme-version distortion. **Form:** If X is accepted, then extreme Y must be accepted. Y is absurd. Therefore X is false. | “You say students should be allowed to ask for deadline extensions in serious emergencies. So you think deadlines should not exist at all.” The extreme version is not implied by the original claim. *Case from the outline: Employees ask for flexibility when they are seriously ill. A manager replies, “So you want nobody to work and deadlines to mean nothing?” The reply attacks an extreme policy nobody proposed.* | As a comparison error, this appears again under M-19. Here the focus is relevance: the reply attacks an extreme substitute instead of the original claim. |
| R-11 Appeal to Stupidity | This fallacy persuades by devaluing reason, data, science, or careful thought. It gets the audience to devalue reason and intellectual discourse, then makes a claim the audience is more likely to accept under that lowered standard of reasoning. | Anti-reason pressure. **Form:** Reason/data are portrayed as elitist or useless. Claim X is made. Audience is encouraged to accept X by feeling rather than judging. | “Experts throw charts at you, but charts do not feed your children. You know in your gut that the policy failed.” The argument discourages evidence evaluation before making the conclusion. | Some choices are properly affective or personal, such as choosing a puppy or a favorite song. The fallacy appears when objective or public claims are shielded from reason by attacking reason itself. |
| R-12 Appeal to Desperation | This fallacy argues that because something must be done, the proposed action must be done. The form is: something must be done; X is something; therefore X must be done. The error is substituting urgency for relevance and adequacy. | Urgency-as-proof error. **Form:** We must act. X is an action. Therefore X is justified. | “The company is losing money. We must do something. Therefore, we should fire half the staff tomorrow.” Urgency does not show that this action is effective or proportionate. | In emergencies, imperfect action may be better than no action. Still, the action needs some reason: probability of success, proportionality, and comparison with alternatives. |
| R-13 Style Over Substance | This fallacy evaluates an argument by its presentation rather than its reasons. It treats elegance, fluency, confidence, humor, anger, academic vocabulary, or poor delivery as if they determined truth. | Presentation-substitution error. **Form:** Claim X is delivered well/badly. Therefore X is true/false. | “Her argument must be right; she spoke calmly, used technical words, and looked authoritative.” Or: “His point is wrong because he stuttered.” Delivery is being substituted for evidence. *Case from the outline: A speaker’s confident posture, teaching tone, clever phrasing, and high-status pose are taken as proof that he has the better argument, even though he has not supplied stronger reasons.* | Style can matter when clarity, credibility, or communication is the issue. But style cannot replace the evaluation of premises, evidence, and inference. |
| R-14 Overextended Outrage as Topic Diversion | This is the relevance variant of overextended outrage. A vivid bad case becomes a dramatic side issue, and attention moves away from the original question toward anger at a whole group. | Outrage-diversion error. **Form:** A bad case is introduced; the discussion shifts from the issue to generalized condemnation. | A hearing about housing policy turns into repeated stories about one fraudulent applicant, without showing that the case represents the policy problem. | The sampling version is listed under I-13. Here the defect is that outrage redirects the discussion. |
| R-15 Hypnotic Bait and Switch | This fallacy gives a sequence of easy, uncontroversial claims, then slips in a controversial claim while the audience is accustomed to agreeing. The propaganda or sales technique is simple: several true statements are followed by the claim the arguer wants accepted. | Agreement-conditioning error. **Form:** True A, true B, true C. Therefore controversial X. | “Do you care about your family? Do you care about safety? Do you want a better future? Then support this surveillance bill.” The earlier “yes” answers do not prove the bill is justified. | Rhetorical sequencing is not automatically fallacious. It becomes fallacious when agreement with harmless claims is transferred to a disputed conclusion without evidence. |
| R-16 Pragmatic Fallacy | This fallacy treats practical benefit from believing a claim as evidence that the claim is true. The formulation is: I believe X; believing X gives practical benefit Y; therefore X is true. The error is confusing usefulness of belief with truth of belief. | Usefulness-to-truth error. **Form:** Believing X helps me. Therefore X is true. | “Astrology is true because reading my horoscope makes me more confident and my day goes better.” The belief may have a psychological effect, but that does not establish astrology’s truth. | Practical benefit can be relevant to whether a belief or practice is useful, therapeutic, motivating, or socially valuable. It does not by itself prove factual truth. |
| R-17 Confusing an Explanation with an Excuse | This fallacy treats an explanation of why something happened as if it were a justification, excuse, or defense of it. The relevance error lies in replacing a normative question with a causal or descriptive account. It treats explanation of a fact as if it justified the fact or proved it true. | Explanation-justification confusion. **Form:** X is explained by Y. Therefore X is justified or acceptable. | “The employee leaked data because he was angry about management.” That may explain the action, but it does not by itself justify leaking data. | Explanation can matter for responsibility, mitigation, prevention, or policy design. It becomes fallacious when explanation is treated as full moral or legal justification. |
| R-18 Gadarene Swine Fallacy | This fallacy assumes that if one person stands apart from the group, the individual must be wrong. It assumes that because someone is not in formation with the group, that individual must be off course; but the apparent outsider may be the one on the right course. | Majority-conformity relevance error. **Form:** X differs from the group. Therefore X is wrong. | “Why can’t your daughter just fall in line like the other students?” The argument assumes conformity is correctness without showing the group is right. | Being different does not make someone right either. If the group has strong evidence, standards, or expertise, deviation may be suspicious. But the conclusion must rest on those reasons, not on conformity alone. |
| R-19 Subverted Support | This fallacy explains a phenomenon that has not been shown to exist. Instead of proving that X occurs, the arguer jumps to explaining why X occurs. It works as a form of begging the question: the audience is encouraged to focus on the cause while accepting the unproven phenomenon. | Unproven-phenomenon explanation. **Form:** X happens because of Y, though X has not been established. | “The reason students are secretly refusing to read books is that video platforms destroyed attention.” Before explaining the cause, one must show the alleged widespread refusal exists. | If X is already well established, explaining X is legitimate. The fallacy appears when the explanation distracts from the need to prove X in the first place. |
| R-20 Appeal to Ridicule | This fallacy makes a position seem false by making it look laughable. Ridicule may lower the audience’s willingness to take the claim seriously, but laughter is not refutation. | Mockery-as-refutation error. **Form:** Claim X is made to appear silly. Therefore X is false. | “You think public transit needs investment? What next, government-issued bicycles and poetry readings on every bus?” The joke displaces the actual policy question. | Satire can expose real contradictions when it accurately targets the argument. It becomes fallacious when mockery replaces evidence or distorts the position. |
| R-21 `Whataboutism` | Whataboutism answers a charge by introducing another wrongdoing, often by the critic, the critic’s group, or a rival side. It overlaps with tu quoque and red herring, but its distinctive move is counter-accusatory topic diversion. | Counter-charge diversion. **Form:** A criticizes B for X. B replies, What about Y? Therefore X is left unanswered. | “Why did your office hide the safety report?” “What about the previous administration’s waste?” The earlier waste may matter elsewhere, but it does not answer the hidden report. *Case from the outline: “This neighborhood is unsafe at night and needs better lighting.” “Other countries are unsafe at night too.” The comparison may be true, but it leaves the local safety claim unanswered.* | Comparisons can be legitimate when the issue is consistency or priority. It becomes fallacious when the comparison is used to avoid the original burden. |

## Personal, Identity, Source, and Global-Discrediting Errors

These fallacies belong together because they shift attention from **the argument** to **the person, group, origin, identity, status, or past record of the arguer**. The core error is not that personal information is always irrelevant. This point requires care: questions about bias, credibility, interest, consistency, or testimony can sometimes be legitimate, especially in inquiry, legal testimony, or expert evaluation. The fallacy appears when personal information is used to dismiss a claim without examining its reasons, or when the personal attack blocks further dialogue by making the speaker’s future arguments automatically suspect.  

| Fallacy                                     | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      | Mechanism                                                                                                                                | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                                     |
| --------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| A-01 `Abusive Ad Hominem` / Argumentum ad Hominem | This fallacy attacks the speaker’s character, intelligence, morality, personality, or social traits instead of answering the argument. The defect is not the insult itself, but the use of the insult as if it refuted the claim. Abusive personal attack often targets honesty, reliability, integrity, credibility, or capacity for reasoned argument. | Personal-attack substitution. **Form:** Person P argues X. P is bad/stupid/corrupt/ridiculous. Therefore X is false. | “Her argument about tax policy is worthless; she is unmarried, unstable, and was once arrested.” None of these facts answers the tax argument. | Character may be relevant when the issue is testimony, trustworthiness, or role fitness. It is fallacious when character is used as a replacement for evaluating the claim. |
| A-02 `Circumstantial Ad Hominem` | This fallacy rejects a claim because of the speaker’s circumstances: job, class, party, religion, financial interest, institutional position, or prior commitments. The error is treating circumstance as disproof. Circumstantial arguments are not always fallacious: sometimes they legitimately test consistency or bias. | Circumstance-as-refutation error. **Form:** P argues X. P has circumstance C. Therefore X is false. | “You work for a public university, so your defense of public education is just self-interest and can be ignored.” The employment fact may invite caution, but does not refute the argument. | If the issue is hidden interest, conflict of interest, or consistency, circumstances may be relevant. The correct conclusion is usually “check the evidence independently,” not “therefore false.” |
| A-03 `Poisoning the Well` / Smear Tactic / Preemptive Ad Hominem | This fallacy discredits a speaker in advance so that anything he later says will be rejected before being heard. It is a preemptive ad hominem that primes the audience with adverse information about the interlocutor. The danger is that it can close off reasonable dialogue altogether. | Preemptive credibility attack. **Form:** Before P speaks, audience is told P is biased/dishonest/irrational. Therefore P’s future arguments are dismissed. | “My opponent will now respond with his usual dishonest statistics, so do not be fooled by anything he says.” The audience is trained to reject the reply before examining it. | Relevant warnings are not always fallacious. If someone has a documented pattern of fabrication on the same issue, that may affect credibility. But it must not replace evaluation of the specific argument. |
| A-04 `Guilt by Association` / Ad Hominem Guilt by Association | This fallacy discredits a claim by associating its speaker with a disliked person, group, institution, or ideology. The defect is assuming that association transfers error or guilt. | Association-as-disproof error. **Form:** P is associated with disliked group G. P argues X. Therefore X is false. | “That historian once spoke at a conference sponsored by a conservative foundation, so his archival argument must be propaganda.” The sponsorship may invite scrutiny, but does not decide the archival evidence. | Association can matter if it shows funding, coordination, conflict of interest, or direct influence. It becomes fallacious when mere connection replaces analysis. |
| A-05 `Genetic Fallacy` | The genetic fallacy evaluates a claim only by its origin rather than by its current evidence, reasoning, or use. The origin may explain how the belief arose, but it does not determine truth or falsity. | Origin-as-truth-value error. **Form:** Claim X came from source/origin O. O is bad/suspicious/old/foreign. Therefore X is false. | “This concept originated in religious debate, so it cannot be useful in modern ethics.” The origin may be historically interesting, but the concept still needs evaluation. | Origin can be relevant to context, motive, or reliability. It is not decisive unless the origin affects the evidence or reasoning now being used. |
| A-06 Psychogenetic Fallacy | This fallacy dismisses a belief by explaining the psychological reason why someone holds it. Even if the psychological explanation is true, it does not settle the truth of the belief. | Psychology-as-refutation error. **Form:** P believes X because of psychological cause Y. Therefore X is false. | “You only believe in justice because you need to feel the world is orderly; therefore justice is an illusion.” The motive may explain the belief, but it does not refute the concept. | Psychological diagnosis can be relevant when assessing reliability or self-deception. But it does not replace analysis of the claim itself. |
