| A-07 Bulverism | Bulverism assumes that an opponent is wrong and then explains why the opponent came to hold the wrong belief. The defect is reversing the proper order: first prove error, then explain its cause. | Assumed-error-plus-explanation. **Form:** P claims X. Instead of refuting X, critic explains why P would believe X. | “You defend free markets because you grew up privileged.” That may be a sociological hypothesis, but it does not show the market argument is false. | If the claim has already been shown false, explaining why someone believed it may be useful. Bulverism occurs when explanation substitutes for refutation. |
| A-08 `Reductio ad Hitlerum` / Playing the Hitler Card | This fallacy rejects a view because it resembles, or is alleged to resemble, something associated with Hitler, Nazis, or another universally condemned figure. The error is guilt by extreme association rather than substantive comparison. | Extreme association error. **Form:** Hitler/Nazis supported or resembled X. Therefore X is false or evil. | “The Nazis promoted physical fitness; therefore modern public fitness campaigns are suspicious.” The association is too broad and irrelevant. | Historical comparison may be legitimate when there are precise structural similarities: authoritarian methods, racial ideology, propaganda techniques, or state violence. Mere association is not enough. |
| A-09 `Scapegoating` | Scapegoating unfairly blames an unpopular, weak, or convenient person or group for a complex problem. It blames an unpopular or easy target for a problem. | Easy-target causation and blame. **Form:** Group G is disliked or vulnerable. Problem Y exists. Therefore G caused Y. | “The city’s economic problems are caused by migrants.” This ignores housing policy, labor markets, capital flows, infrastructure, and governance. *Case from the outline: A project fails after vague goals, poor planning, and weak supervision. Instead of examining those causes, the leader blames the youngest employee or the child in the room because that person is easiest to punish.* | A group or institution can be causally involved in a problem if evidence shows it. The fallacy lies in assigning blame because the target is convenient, not because the evidence supports it. |
| A-10 `Stereotyping` | This fallacy attributes traits to an individual because of group membership. It treats a group-level image, whether true, false, or exaggerated, as sufficient evidence about a particular person. | Group-to-individual overreach. **Form:** Members of group G are said to have trait T. P belongs to G. Therefore P has T. | “He studied philosophy, so he must be impractical and bad with numbers.” The group stereotype is used instead of evidence about the individual. | Statistical tendencies can sometimes inform risk assessment, but they must be handled carefully and cannot replace individual evidence in moral, legal, or intellectual evaluation. |
| A-11 Identity Fallacy | This fallacy treats identity as if it automatically determines truth, moral standing, or interpretive authority. It may say that only members of a group can be right about an issue, or that members of a group must hold a certain view. | Identity-as-truth error. **Form:** P has identity I. Therefore P’s claim is true/false, or P must believe X. | “You cannot criticize this policy because you are not from the affected community.” Lived experience may matter, but the policy argument still requires reasons. | Identity can provide situated knowledge and reveal overlooked evidence. It becomes fallacious when identity alone is treated as conclusive proof or disproof. |
| A-12 Tokenism | Tokenism uses one member of a group as decisive evidence about the group as a whole, or as a shield against criticism. It turns one person’s identity into a substitute for broader evidence. | Token-as-proof error. **Form:** One member of group G supports X. Therefore X is good for G, or criticism from G is invalid. | “One woman on our board supports the policy, so the policy cannot be sexist.” One person’s support does not settle group-level effects. | Testimony from group members can be important evidence. The fallacy lies in treating one selected example as final or representative without justification. |
| A-13 Traitorous Critic Fallacy | This fallacy dismisses criticism of one’s own group, nation, party, tradition, or institution by calling the critic disloyal. The error is replacing the question “Is the criticism true?” with “Is the critic loyal?” | Loyalty-as-silencing error. **Form:** P criticizes group G. P belongs to or benefits from G. Therefore P is a traitor and the criticism can be ignored. | “You studied at this university, so criticizing its admissions system is betrayal.” The criticism may be right or wrong; loyalty does not decide it. | Some criticism may be unfair, opportunistic, or malicious. But that must be shown by evidence, not by treating criticism itself as disloyalty. |
| A-14 Nutpicking as Group Discredit | This is the personal-discrediting variant of nutpicking. It selects the most irrational or embarrassing members of a group in order to discredit the group or movement as a whole. | Extreme-member discrediting. **Form:** Marginal member F belongs to group G. F is absurd. Therefore G is absurd. | A critic presents one incoherent activist comment as proof that the entire movement is intellectually bankrupt. | The sampling version is listed under I-12. Here the focus is global discrediting through a selected person. |
| A-15 Appeal to Accomplishment | This fallacy treats a person’s achievement in one area as proof that his claim in another area is true. Accomplishment creates prestige, not universal expertise. | Achievement-transfer error. **Form:** P succeeded in field A. P claims X in field B. Therefore X is true. | “He built a billion-dollar company, so his views on ancient history must be correct.” Business success does not transfer automatically to historical expertise. *Case from the outline: A veteran says that anyone with more years of experience must be better at every task, regardless of whether the task requires different methods, current knowledge, or actual performance evidence.* | Accomplishment is relevant when it is in the same field and connected to the claim. It can justify attention, not automatic acceptance. |
| A-16 Appeal to Common Folk | This fallacy treats ordinary background, plain speech, or non-elite status as evidence of truth or moral correctness. It is the populist mirror of appeal to elite status. | Ordinary-person-as-proof error. **Form:** P is ordinary/common/not elite. Therefore P’s claim is true or morally superior. | “He has no fancy education, so he tells the truth about economics.” Lack of elite status does not establish expertise or accuracy. | Ordinary experience can reveal problems experts miss. The fallacy lies in treating commonness itself as proof. |
| A-17 Argument from Age | This fallacy evaluates a claim by the age of the arguer. It may assume that older people are wiser or that younger people are more innovative, without examining the argument. | Age-as-credibility shortcut. **Form:** P is old/young. Therefore P’s claim is true/false. | “She is only twenty-two, so her argument about constitutional law cannot be serious.” Age does not determine the quality of the legal reasoning. | Age may matter when the issue is life experience, developmental capacity, or historical memory. It is not a general substitute for evidence. |
| A-18 Argument to the Purse / Argumentum ad Crumenam / Appeal to Wealth or Poverty | This fallacy judges a claim by the arguer’s wealth or poverty. It concludes truth or falsity from financial status: wealth is taken as wisdom, or poverty is taken as purity. | Financial-status-as-truth error. **Form:** P is rich/poor. P says X. Therefore X is true/false. | “This investor is extremely rich, so his theory of education must be right.” Or: “This monk owns nothing, so his view of society must be pure.” | Wealth can be relevant when the claim concerns financial success, market experience, or conflicts of interest. It cannot decide unrelated truth claims. |
| A-19 Trust or Distrust as Personal Discredit | This is the personal or identity-based variant of appeal to trust and distrust. A person’s affiliation, reputation, or source identity is used to accept or dismiss a claim without examining the claim itself. | Source-identity shortcut. **Form:** Speaker S belongs to trusted or distrusted group G. Therefore S’s claim is true or false. | “She works for that institute, so everything she says about the policy is propaganda.” The affiliation may call for scrutiny, but it does not settle the claim. | The evidence-source version is listed under S-08. Here the focus is how source identity becomes a global personal discredit. |
| A-20 Argument by Personal Charm | This fallacy accepts a claim because the speaker is charming, warm, charismatic, attractive, funny, or personally likable. The audience confuses interpersonal appeal with evidential strength. | Charm-as-evidence error. **Form:** P is charming. P says X. Therefore X is true. | A startup founder gives an inspiring speech; investors conclude the product must be technically sound without checking the prototype. | Personal presentation may matter for leadership, communication, or trust-building. It does not prove factual, technical, or moral claims. |
| A-21 Appeal to Motive | This fallacy rejects a claim because the speaker has a motive for making it. Motive can raise a question about bias, but motive is not disproof. | Motive-as-refutation error. **Form:** P benefits from X or wants X. Therefore X is false. | “Doctors recommend vaccines because they profit from healthcare, so vaccines must be ineffective.” Profit motives may justify scrutiny, not the conclusion of ineffectiveness. | Motive is relevant to conflict of interest and credibility. The proper response is independent verification, not automatic rejection. |
| A-22 Ad Fidentia / Attack on Confidence | This fallacy tries to undermine an opponent by attacking the opponent’s confidence, competence, or right to speak, rather than the argument. It often works by making the opponent feel unqualified before the reasons are examined. | Confidence-undermining attack. **Form:** P lacks confidence/status/credentials. Therefore P should not be heard, or X is false. | “You are clearly not confident enough to defend this position, so your claim about the data must be wrong.” The emotional pressure does not address the data. | Lack of competence may be relevant if the person is presented as an expert. But ordinary arguments can still be evaluated independently of the speaker’s confidence. |
| A-23 `From One Error to Total Error` / 由一错推全错 | This fallacy infers from one mistake to the rejection of a person’s entire body of thought. The error is overextension: a local error does not automatically contaminate every other claim. | Local-error-to-total-discredit error. **Form:** P was wrong about X. Therefore P is wrong about everything, or all of P’s theories are useless. | “He made one factual error about this historical event, so all his literary theory is worthless.” The local error may require correction, not total dismissal. | If the error lies in a foundational premise on which the whole system depends, broader rejection may be justified. The critic must show the dependency. |
| A-24 From One Error to Total Incompetence / 由单一错误否定整体能力 | This fallacy turns one mistaken judgment into a global judgment about a person’s intelligence, expertise, or capacity for thought. It confuses error with incompetence. | Single-mistake-to-global-ability error. **Form:** P made error E. Therefore P lacks ability A. | “This article misuses one philosophical term; therefore the author does not understand philosophy at all.” The mistake may show a gap, not total inability. | Repeated serious errors in a core area can support doubts about competence. A single error usually cannot bear that weight. |
| A-25 From Local Defect to Collapse of Theory / 由局部缺陷否定整个理论体系 | This fallacy assumes that because one part of a theory is defective, the entire theory collapses. It fails to ask whether the defect is peripheral, revisable, or foundational. | Part-to-whole discrediting error. **Form:** Theory T has defect D. Therefore T is false as a whole. | “This social theory has an outdated claim about family structure, so its analysis of capital, class, and institutions is also wrong.” The defect may affect one module, not the whole system. | If the defective part is a core premise, central definition, or indispensable inference, the whole theory may indeed be damaged. The burden is to show that structural dependence. |
| A-26 From Past Error to Present Falsehood / 由过去错误否定现在观点 | This fallacy rejects a present argument because the speaker was wrong in the past. A past error may affect trust, but it does not decide the present argument. | Past-record-as-automatic-refutation error. **Form:** P was wrong before. P now argues X. Therefore X is false. | “He predicted a recession wrongly ten years ago, so his current inflation analysis must be wrong.” The new claim needs its own evaluation. | Track record matters when assessing reliability, especially in forecasting. But it changes prior confidence; it does not replace analysis of current evidence. |
| A-27 `Just-World Fallacy` / Victim-Blaming Fallacy | This fallacy treats harm as evidence that the harmed person must have caused, invited, or deserved it. It preserves a belief that the world is fair by shifting responsibility toward the victim before facts about power, coercion, accident, or asymmetry are examined. | Deserved-harm attribution. **Form:** X suffered harm. Therefore X must have caused, invited, or deserved the harm. | *Case from the outline: After someone reports being hurt or exploited, bystanders say, “it takes two hands to clap,” as if the mere existence of conflict proved equal responsibility. The response turns a report of harm into suspicion of the harmed person.* | Victims can make choices that matter for prevention or context, but that does not by itself assign blame. Responsibility must be based on evidence about agency, power, intent, and alternatives. |

## Authority, Expert, Testimony, and Evidence-Source Errors

These fallacies belong together because they mishandle **mediated knowledge**: things we know through experts, institutions, documents, testimony, citation, journalism, research, or reported evidence. The central mistake is not “using authority.” Appeal to expert opinion is a legitimate but defeasible argumentation scheme: an expert in domain D asserts A, A falls within D, therefore A may plausibly be accepted, subject to critical questions about expertise, field, exact opinion, trustworthiness, expert agreement, and backup evidence.  The boundary is clear: deferring provisionally to relevant experts is often reasonable; the fallacy occurs when authority replaces evidence, or when the authority is irrelevant, anonymous, uncheckable, or used to block criticism.

| Fallacy                                                 | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Mechanism                                                                                                                   | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                                 |
| --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| S-01 `Appeal to Authority` / Argumentum ad Verecundiam / Argument from Authority / Ipse Dixit | This fallacy treats an authority’s statement as if it conclusively proved a claim. The error is not citing experts, but using expert opinion as a substitute for evidence, method, and critical questions. The correct status is clear: expert opinion gives defeasible, presumptive support, not final proof. | Authority-as-proof error. **Form:** Expert E says X. Therefore X is true. | “A famous evolutionary biologist says evolution is true; therefore evolution is true.” The conclusion may be true, but not because one expert says so. It is supported by the evidence, methods, and convergence of the field. *Case from the outline: In a factual dispute, one side quotes a famous leader or scholar and treats the quotation as higher than the available facts and logic. The prestige of the name replaces the missing argument.* | Relevant expert opinion is reasonable when the expert is credible, the claim lies within the expert’s field, the opinion is accurately reported, other experts broadly agree, and evidence supports it. |
| S-02 `False Authority` / Appeal to Doubtful Authority / Appeal to Irrelevant Authority        | This fallacy uses someone as an authority when that person lacks relevant expertise on the issue. The speaker may be intelligent, famous, sincere, successful, or trained in another field, but that does not make the person an authority here. It uses an alleged authority when the authority is not really an authority on the relevant facts.  | Irrelevant-expertise error. **Form:** Person P has prestige in field A. P makes claim X in field B. Therefore X is credible.                  | “A Nobel physicist says this diet prevents cancer, so it must be true.” Physics expertise does not automatically transfer to oncology or nutrition.                                                                            | Cross-field judgment may matter when the person has relevant interdisciplinary evidence or training. But prestige alone is not transferable authority.                                                   |
| S-03 `Anonymous Authority` / Appeal to Anonymous Authority                                    | This fallacy cites unnamed experts, unnamed studies, insiders, researchers, or “people in the field” without enough information to evaluate them. Vague expert references such as “according to the experts” prevent questions about who the experts are and what their field is.                                                        | Uncheckable-source error. **Form:** Experts say X. Therefore X is true.                                                                       | “Experts say that this supplement repairs the immune system.” Which experts? In what field? On what evidence? With what study design? The appeal cannot be evaluated.                                                          | Anonymous sources can be legitimate in journalism or whistleblowing when institutions provide verification, corroboration, and accountability. Mere anonymity plus authority language is not enough.     |
| S-04 `Blind Authority Fallacy` / Blind Obedience / Team Player Appeal / Nuremberg Defense     | This fallacy treats an authority as ultimate and unchallengeable, so that counterevidence is ignored and questioning itself is treated as disobedience. It asserts a proposition solely on the authority making the claim, often while ignoring counterevidence.                                                                                 | Obedience-as-truth error. **Form:** Authority A commands or asserts X. A must be obeyed. Therefore X is true or right.                        | “The commander ordered it, so the act was justified.” The fact of command does not settle moral, legal, or factual justification.                                                                                              | Some institutions require obedience for coordination and safety, but obedience does not make a claim true or an act morally justified. Authority must still be constrained by law, ethics, and evidence. |
| S-05 `Appeal to Celebrity` / Celebrity Endorsement                                            | This fallacy accepts a claim because a celebrity endorses it. Celebrity creates visibility and emotional attraction, not relevant expertise. A famous actor recommending a product shows the structure: the celebrity may be admired, but admiration does not prove the claim.                                                                     | Fame-as-evidence error. **Form:** Celebrity C says product or claim X is good/true. Therefore X is good/true.                                 | “A film star says this skin treatment reverses aging, so it must work.” The star’s personal charisma and market visibility do not establish dermatological efficacy.                                                           | Celebrity testimony may be relevant to personal experience, taste, or products directly tied to the celebrity’s field. It is not scientific or technical proof.                                          |
| S-06 Appeal to Faith                                                                          | This fallacy uses faith as a substitute for reasons in a context where reasons are required. The pattern is clear: when asked for evidence, the speaker replies that one must simply have faith.                                                                                                                                                         | Faith-as-evidence error. **Form:** X is true. If you have faith, you will see that X is true.                                                 | “How do you know this hidden cosmic history is real?” “I know through faith; if you cannot see it, your faith is weak.” The reply does not provide public reasons.                                                             | Faith may be meaningful as personal commitment, religious practice, or existential orientation. It becomes fallacious when used to justify a public factual claim while refusing evidence.               |
| S-07 Appeal to Heaven / Divine Authority Fallacy                                              | This fallacy treats divine endorsement, providence, sacred authority, or heavenly approval as sufficient proof of a contested claim. It often overlaps with appeal to faith and blind authority.                                                                                                                                                                        | Sacred-source-as-proof error. **Form:** God / heaven / sacred source supports X. Therefore X is true or right.                                | “This policy must be right because it is the will of heaven.” The argument gives no independent way to test either the claimed divine will or its application to the policy.                                                   | In theological reasoning, sacred authority may be an accepted premise inside that community. In public argument with people who do not share that premise, further reasons are needed.                   |
| S-08 Appeal to Trust / Appeal to Distrust                                                     | This fallacy treats trusted sources as automatically true or distrusted sources as automatically false. Trust becomes a shortcut that replaces examination. The general boundary is that each claim should still be evaluated on its own merits.                                                                                                                  | Source-trust absolutism. **Form:** I trust source S. S says X. Therefore X is true. Or: I distrust S. S says X. Therefore X is false.         | “My favorite newspaper reported it, so it must be accurate.” Or: “That outlet is biased, so every fact it reports is false.”                                                                                                   | Trust can rationally affect initial confidence when based on expertise, record, and accountability. It should change how carefully we verify, not replace verification.                                  |
| S-09 Argument from Hearsay | This fallacy uses secondhand or thirdhand report as if it were direct evidence. The problem is not that hearsay is always false, but that each transmission can distort, omit, exaggerate, or misremember. | Unverified-testimony chain. **Form:** Someone told someone that X. Therefore X is true. | “A friend of my cousin heard that the company is about to collapse, so we should sell all shares.” The claim may be worth checking, but the chain is too weak to treat as evidence. *Case from the outline: A health claim spreads through relatives and friend groups in a confident pseudo-scientific essay. The fact that trusted acquaintances shared it is treated as evidence that the claim is reliable.* | Hearsay can trigger investigation. It becomes stronger only when traced to reliable, direct, documented, or independently corroborated sources. |
| S-10 `False Attribution`                                                                      | False attribution assigns a claim, quotation, idea, or statistic to a source that did not actually say it, or assigns it without adequate verification. This corrupts evidence at the source level: the authority being invoked may not exist for that claim.                                                                                                           | Misattributed-source error. **Form:** Source S said X. Therefore X has authority. But S did not say X, or attribution is unsupported.         | “Einstein said imagination is more important than knowledge, so my scientific claim does not need evidence.” Even if the quote is real, it may be misused; if the attribution is false, the evidence collapses even earlier.   | Correct attribution still does not prove the claim. Attribution only establishes who said it; the argument must still show why the statement matters.                                                    |
| S-11 `Contextomy` / Quoting Out of Context / 断章取义 | Contextomy quotes a source in a way that changes the original meaning. A sentence may be ironic, hypothetical, concessive, quoted from an opponent, or immediately rejected in the next sentence. Removing context turns a source into false evidence. | Context-removal distortion. **Form:** Source says “X” in context meaning not-X or qualified-X. Quoter presents it as unqualified X. | A report says, “Some initially believed the treatment was effective, but later trials failed to confirm this.” A speaker quotes only: “the treatment was effective.” The quotation reverses the source. *Case from the outline: A sentence from an admired figure is lifted from its original context and stretched to settle a modern technical dispute. The quotation is doing work the original passage did not do.* | Short quotation is acceptable if it preserves meaning. The critic must ask whether omitted words change the argumentative role of the quoted passage. |
| S-12 `Argument from Silence` / Silent Evidence                                                | This fallacy infers too much from the absence of mention. Silence may mean absence, but it may also mean lost records, irrelevant context, censorship, limited scope, or ordinary omission.                                                                                                                                                                             | Silence-as-proof error. **Form:** Source S does not mention X. Therefore X did not happen or is false.                                        | “This travel diary does not mention street crime, so there was no street crime in the city.” The diary may not have aimed to record crime.                                                                                     | Silence can be evidence when the source would very likely have mentioned X if X had occurred. The strength depends on genre, scope, preservation, and expectation of mention.                            |
| S-13 `Argument from Ignorance` / Argumentum ad Ignorantiam                                    | This fallacy treats lack of proof as proof of the opposite. The point is nuanced: argument from ignorance can be reasonable as weak, defeasible reasoning when the search is thorough and the burden of proof fits the context, but becomes fallacious when treated as conclusive.                                                                               | Lack-of-evidence overreach. **Form:** X has not been proven false. Therefore X is true. Or: X has not been proven true. Therefore X is false. | “No one has disproved that a secret device controls the weather; therefore it exists.” Absence of disproof is not evidence of existence.                                                                                       | In safety contexts, lack of knowledge can justify precaution: if you do not know a rifle is unloaded, presume it may be loaded. This is a practical presumption, not conclusive proof.                   |
| S-14 `Proof Surrogate`                                                                        | This fallacy uses words that sound like proof while offering no proof. Phrases such as “obviously,” “undeniably,” “everyone knows,” “it is an indisputable fact,” or “the evidence is clear” can function as substitutes for actual evidence. A proof surrogate is a claim masquerading as proof when no proof is being offered.                          | Assertion-disguised-as-evidence. **Form:** X is asserted with proof-like language. No evidence is supplied. Therefore X is treated as proven. | “The minister is corrupt. This is an undeniable fact known by everyone except his supporters.” The phrase “undeniable fact” replaces evidence.                                                                                 | Some facts are genuinely obvious in ordinary contexts. But when a claim is disputed, proof-like wording cannot replace proof.                                                                            |
| S-15 Missing Data Fallacy                                                                     | This fallacy draws a conclusion from an evidence set while ignoring absent data that could change the conclusion. The defect lies not in the data used, but in the data field being incomplete in a relevant way.                                                                                                                                                       | Incomplete-evidence error. **Form:** Available data D supports X. Missing data M is ignored. Therefore X is overclaimed.                      | “Customer reviews show everyone loves the product.” But refunded customers were not surveyed, deleted reviews were excluded, and only five-star buyers received follow-up emails.                                              | No dataset is complete. The fallacy appears when missingness is systematic and relevant, yet treated as if it did not matter.                                                                            |
| S-16 `Willed Ignorance`                                                                       | Willed ignorance refuses to examine conflicting information in order to preserve an existing belief. It refuses to change one’s mind or consider contrary evidence because one wants to maintain one’s current belief.                                                                                                                            | Refusal-to-check error. **Form:** I believe X. You have evidence against X. I refuse to look. Therefore X remains acceptable.                 | “Do not send me the safety reports. I already know the factory is fine.” The belief is protected by avoiding evidence.                                                                                                         | People may sometimes avoid information for psychological self-protection. It becomes an argumentative fallacy when refusal is used to maintain a public claim.                                           |
| S-17 Limited Scope                                                                            | This fallacy uses evidence that covers only a narrow domain but presents the conclusion as if it applied broadly. The source may be reliable inside its scope, yet the conclusion extends beyond it.                                                                                                                                                                    | Scope-overextension error. **Form:** Evidence supports X within domain D. Therefore X holds generally.                                        | “A study of first-year students at one university shows this learning method works; therefore it works for all learners in all education systems.”                                                                             | Narrow evidence can support narrow conclusions. The problem is not limited scope itself, but hiding the limitation.                                                                                      |
| S-18 Limited Depth                                                                            | This fallacy relies on evidence that touches the surface of a topic but does not investigate deeply enough to support the conclusion. It may answer “what appears to be happening” without reaching cause, mechanism, reliability, or context.                                                                                                                          | Shallow-investigation error. **Form:** A superficial check finds X. Therefore a deep conclusion Y is drawn.                                   | “I searched the first page of results and found no criticism of this company; therefore the company has no serious controversy.” The search is not deep enough.                                                                | Shallow checks are acceptable for low-stakes preliminary judgments. Strong claims need deeper search, especially when contrary evidence may be hard to find.                                             |
| S-19 Deceptive Sharing                                                                        | This fallacy shares a source, headline, screenshot, clip, or excerpt in a way that creates a misleading evidential impression. The source may be real, but the act of sharing distorts what the source supports.                                                                                                                                                        | Misleading-source presentation. **Form:** Share artifact A. Audience infers X. But A does not actually support X in context.                  | A user posts a screenshot of a study title suggesting “coffee prevents disease,” omitting that the study found only a weak correlation in a small subgroup.                                                                    | Sharing partial material is not always deceptive. The issue is whether the presentation invites a false inference about what the source shows.                                                           |
| S-20 Argument by Selective Reading                                                            | This fallacy reads only the parts of a source that support one’s position while ignoring qualifications, counterarguments, limitations, or later revisions in the same source.                                                                                                                                                                                          | Selective-source extraction. **Form:** Source S contains A, B, and C. Speaker cites A while ignoring B and C that weaken the conclusion.      | A report says a policy improved short-term employment but increased long-term instability. A politician cites only the employment improvement and says the policy was a success.                                               | Selective focus is legitimate when the cited part is the only relevant part. It becomes fallacious when omitted parts directly change the strength or meaning of the evidence.                           |
| S-21 Alphabet Soup                                                                            | This fallacy overuses acronyms, abbreviations, titles, or technical labels to appear knowledgeable or to intimidate the audience. It is the deliberate excessive use of acronyms and abbreviations to seem more expert or confuse others.                                                                                                                   | Jargon-as-authority error. **Form:** Speaker uses many acronyms/credentials. Audience infers expertise or truth.                              | “Our AI-SOC uses XDR, SIEM, SOAR, UEBA, CNAPP and ZTNA, so our security strategy is obviously mature.” The acronyms do not prove the system works.                                                                             | Technical shorthand is necessary among specialists. The fallacy appears when jargon is used to replace explanation or exploit the audience’s insecurity.                                                 |

## Emotion, Pressure, Group Appeal, and Rhetorical Manipulation Errors

These fallacies belong together because they use **feeling, pressure, belonging, urgency, moral display, or rhetorical momentum** in place of adequate support. The mistake is not that emotion appears in argument. Emotion can reveal stakes, urgency, suffering, danger, loyalty, or injustice. Emotional appeals require care because not every appeal to emotion is fallacious. Appeals to fear, pity, force, and popular feeling become fallacious when they distract from the issue, close dialogue, replace evidence, or pressure the audience into acceptance without meeting the burden of proof.

| Fallacy                   | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Mechanism                                                                                                             | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                       | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                                           |
| --------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| E-01 `Appeal to Emotion` / Argumentum ad Passiones | This fallacy tries to make the audience accept a conclusion by producing an emotional response rather than providing relevant reasons. The emotion may be fear, pity, anger, guilt, shame, pride, hope, disgust, loyalty, or belonging. The error is not emotion itself, but emotion doing the work that evidence should do. | Emotion-as-proof error. **Form:** X makes you feel emotion E. Therefore X is true, right, or should be accepted. | “Look at these heartbreaking images; therefore this exact policy proposal must be adopted.” The suffering may be real, but the specific policy still needs evidence of effectiveness, cost, and alternatives. *Case from the outline: A person responds to criticism by saying, “Your words hurt me, so your criticism is wrong.” The hurt may be real, but it does not decide whether the criticism is accurate.* | Emotion is legitimate when it helps identify what matters. It becomes fallacious when emotional force replaces evidence, causal analysis, or practical comparison. |
| E-02 Argument by Emotive Language / Loaded Emotional Language | This fallacy uses emotionally charged words to smuggle evaluation into description. The words are selected to make acceptance or rejection feel natural before the issue is argued. | Emotion-loaded framing. **Form:** X is described with emotionally loaded term T. Audience absorbs evaluation before reasoning begins. | “The reform is a bureaucratic assault on hardworking families.” The phrase may be rhetorically powerful, but it has not yet shown what the reform does or whether it is unjust. | Strong words are not always wrong. If evidence establishes cruelty, fraud, violence, or exploitation, strong language may be accurate. The fallacy lies in using emotional wording before earning the evaluation. |
| E-03 `Appeal to Fear` / Scare Tactics / Argumentum ad Metum | This fallacy uses fear to push acceptance of a conclusion without showing that the feared consequence is likely, causally connected, or proportionate. Fear appeals differ from threats: fear appeal warns about danger; threat imposes danger if the listener refuses. | Fear-as-proof error. **Form:** If you reject X, terrible Y may happen. Therefore accept X. | “If we do not pass this bill immediately, society will collapse.” The argument needs intermediate mechanisms, probability, and evidence. Fear alone is not proof. | Fear can be reasonable when tied to real risk: public health warnings, disaster preparation, or safety advice. The fallacy appears when the danger is exaggerated, vague, unsupported, or used to bypass scrutiny. |
| E-04 `Appeal to Pity` / Argumentum ad Misericordiam | This fallacy uses compassion to secure a conclusion that requires independent evidence. Pity may be relevant to mercy, assistance, sentencing, or accommodation, but it cannot prove factual innocence, quality, truth, or entitlement by itself. The student-grade example captures the point: hardship may deserve sympathy, but it does not prove the paper deserves an A. | Pity-as-proof error. **Form:** P suffers or struggles. Therefore P’s claim should be accepted, or P deserves the requested result. | “Please give my essay the highest grade; I worked under terrible stress this week.” Stress may justify an extension or compassionate treatment, but not automatically a high grade. | Pity is relevant when the decision explicitly concerns mercy, relief, accommodation, or mitigation. It becomes fallacious when used to settle a factual or evaluative question requiring evidence. |
| E-05 `Appeal to Anger` / Appeal to Outrage | This fallacy uses anger to make a claim seem proven or morally unquestionable. Anger may reveal a real injury, but it can also shut down inquiry by making questions look like betrayal or corruption. | Anger-as-certainty error. **Form:** X is outrageous. Therefore X is true, or no further evidence is needed. | “The accusation is horrifying; only a corrupt person would ask for more evidence.” The horror of the accusation does not prove it true. | Anger can be appropriate when harm is established. The fallacy appears when anger is used to replace investigation, proportionality, or proof. |
| E-06 Appeal to Spite | This fallacy persuades by appealing to resentment, revenge, or the desire to harm someone disliked. The audience is moved to accept a policy or claim because it injures an enemy, not because it is justified. | Resentment-as-reason error. **Form:** Action X hurts disliked group/person Y. Therefore X is good or true. | “This rule may be inefficient, but it will punish the people who mocked us, so we should support it.” Revenge is being used as the reason. | Accountability and sanction can be legitimate when proportionate and justified. The fallacy lies in making spite itself the reason. |
| E-07 Appeal to Flattery | This fallacy manipulates the audience by praising them, implying that smart, moral, patriotic, sophisticated, or independent people will accept the conclusion. The praise creates pressure to agree. | Flattery-pressure error. **Form:** You are admirable if you accept X. Therefore accept X. | “As intelligent readers, you will of course see that my proposal is the only rational one.” The audience is invited to agree in order to preserve self-image. | Respecting an audience is not fallacious. It becomes manipulative when praise substitutes for argument or makes disagreement feel shameful. |
| E-08 `Appeal to Force` / Argumentum ad Baculum / Threat Appeal | This fallacy uses threat or coercion as if it supported a conclusion. It replaces persuasion with pressure. The distinction is nuanced: in persuasion dialogue, threats are normally irrelevant; in negotiation, consequences may be part of bargaining. The fallacy appears when force is offered where reasons are required. | Coercion-as-reason error. **Form:** Accept X, or I will impose cost Y. Therefore X is true or should be accepted. | “Admit that my interpretation is correct, or you will lose your job.” The threat does not support the interpretation. | Legal sanctions, bargaining consequences, and safety warnings are not automatically fallacious. The issue is whether the threat is being used as a reason for truth or justification. |
| E-09 Appeal to Loyalty | This fallacy pressures someone to accept a claim or policy because loyalty allegedly requires it. It replaces the evaluation of reasons with a demand for allegiance. | Loyalty-as-proof error. **Form:** If you are loyal to group G, you must accept X. | “A real member of this company would support management’s decision without asking questions.” The appeal to loyalty blocks evaluation of the decision. | Loyalty can matter in roles, relationships, and collective action. It becomes fallacious when it forbids legitimate criticism or replaces evidence. |
| E-10 `Appeal to Popularity` / Bandwagon / Argumentum ad Populum | This fallacy treats popularity as proof. Many people believe or support X, therefore X is true, good, or justified. The core error in appeal to common belief is that the number of believers does not supply the reasons for the belief. | Popularity-as-truth error. **Form:** Many people believe X. Therefore X is true. | “Millions of users share this health advice, so it must work.” Sharing and belief do not establish medical effectiveness. *Case from the outline: In a fandom dispute, the side that repeats a slogan most loudly is treated as the side with the truth. The number and volume of voices substitute for standards of evidence.* | Popularity can be evidence of social meaning, market demand, or public sentiment. It does not by itself prove factual truth or moral rightness. |
| E-11 Appeal to Common Belief | This fallacy is close to appeal to popularity but emphasizes belief rather than social trend. It assumes that a widely held belief is probably true merely because it is widely held. | Shared-belief-as-proof error. **Form:** It is commonly believed that X. Therefore X is true. | “For centuries many people believed the earth was the center of the universe; therefore they had good reason to think so.” Widespread belief does not equal evidence. | Common belief may preserve inherited knowledge in some domains. It becomes fallacious when the belief is not checked against evidence, expertise, or method. |
| E-12 `Appeal to Common Sense` | This fallacy treats “common sense” as a final argument. The phrase often hides unexamined assumptions, local experience, social prejudice, or impatience with complexity. | Common-sense-as-closure error. **Form:** X is common sense. Therefore X needs no evidence. | “It is just common sense that harsher punishment always reduces crime.” This is an empirical claim requiring evidence. | Common sense can be a useful starting point in ordinary life. It becomes fallacious when it stops inquiry or substitutes for data in complex questions. |
| E-13 Argument by Fast Talking | This fallacy overwhelms the listener by speed, density, and verbal momentum. The goal is not clarity but preventing careful evaluation. It resembles rhetorical pressure rather than reason. | Speed-pressure error. **Form:** Speaker rapidly presents many claims. Listener cannot inspect them. Claims seem stronger than they are. | A salesperson floods a customer with financing terms, technical jargon, and urgent deadlines until the customer signs without understanding. | Fast explanation is not automatically fallacious when the audience understands it. It becomes fallacious when speed is used to prevent scrutiny. |
| E-14 Argument by Gibberish | This fallacy uses confusing, obscure, or meaningless language to create the impression of depth. The audience may accept the claim because they cannot parse it and assume the speaker is profound. | Obscurity-as-depth error. **Form:** Speaker uses opaque language. Audience mistakes incomprehension for sophistication. | “The product aligns your quantum intention matrix with bio-harmonic prosperity fields.” The language sounds technical but offers no evaluable claim. | Technical language may be necessary in specialized fields. The fallacy lies in using obscurity to avoid explanation. |
| E-15 Argument by Repetition / Ad Nauseam | This fallacy treats repeated assertion as if repetition increased evidence. A claim becomes familiar, memorable, and socially available, but not better supported. | Repetition-as-proof error. **Form:** X is repeated many times. Therefore X is accepted as true. | “This policy is a disaster” is repeated in every speech, ad, and interview, without evidence. Listeners begin to feel it must be true. | Repetition can help communication and memory. It becomes fallacious when repetition substitutes for reasons. |
| E-16 Argument by Pigheadedness | This fallacy treats stubborn refusal to change one’s mind as strength. The speaker presents immovability as evidence of conviction, while ignoring objections or counterevidence. | Stubbornness-as-credibility error. **Form:** I will never change my mind. Therefore my position is strong. | “No matter what studies you show, I will always know this treatment is fake.” The refusal is not an argument. | Holding firm can be rational when evidence is strong and objections are weak. Pigheadedness appears when refusal replaces engagement. |
| E-17 Proof by Intimidation | This fallacy attempts to win agreement through social or intellectual intimidation. The audience is made to feel stupid, cowardly, immoral, or unqualified if they ask for proof. | Intimidation-as-proof error. **Form:** Only fools question X. Therefore X is accepted without evidence. | “Anyone who does not understand why this theory is obvious should not be in this room.” The pressure discourages questions. *Case from the outline: A speaker begins with “Wrong. Listen carefully,” then keeps using the posture of a judge or teacher until the other person stops asking for reasons. The intimidation produces compliance, not proof.* | Expertise-based correction is not intimidation if it explains the issue. The fallacy lies in making shame or fear do the work of proof. |
| E-18 Appeal to Closure as Emotional Pressure | This fallacy uses fatigue, discomfort, or the desire for emotional relief as a reason to accept a conclusion or stop considering objections. It pressures the audience to end the issue because continued discussion feels bad. | Relief-as-proof error. **Form:** Continuing discussion is tiring or painful. Therefore the preferred conclusion should be accepted. | “Everyone is exhausted by this investigation, so we should accept the official story and move on.” Fatigue does not answer the evidence. | The procedural version is listed under D-16. Here the focus is emotional pressure rather than formal closure of a dialogue. |
| E-19 Appeal to Equality | This fallacy uses the positive emotional force of equality to support a conclusion without showing how equality applies. It may treat unequal cases as if equal treatment were automatically required, or treat any difference as injustice. | Equality-label-as-proof error. **Form:** X is called equality. Therefore X is right. | “Every student should receive the same grade because equality matters.” Equal grading ignores different performance. | Equality is a serious moral principle. The issue is what kind of equality is relevant: equal rights, equal opportunity, equal treatment, equal outcome, or equal respect. |
| E-20 Notable Effort | This fallacy treats effort as proof of quality, truth, or entitlement. Effort may deserve respect, but it does not guarantee success or correctness. | Effort-as-result error. **Form:** P worked hard on X. Therefore X is good, true, or deserves reward R. | “I spent six months writing this essay, so it deserves a top grade.” Effort may matter in feedback or sympathy, but quality still needs evaluation. | Effort can be relevant in formative assessment, moral appraisal, or compensation for labor. It does not automatically establish truth or quality. |
| E-21 Righteousness Fallacy | This fallacy treats the moral attractiveness of one’s cause as proof that one’s reasoning is sound. Because the speaker stands for justice, compassion, truth, nation, or progress, the specific argument is treated as beyond criticism. | Moral-position-as-proof error. **Form:** My cause is righteous. Therefore my claim or method is correct. | “We fight for the oppressed, so our statistics do not need to be checked by hostile outsiders.” A good cause does not make evidence immune. | Moral commitments can legitimately guide inquiry and priorities. They become fallacious when they exempt arguments from scrutiny. |
| E-22 Self-Righteousness Fallacy | This fallacy treats one’s own moral purity as evidence. It shifts attention from the claim to the speaker’s self-image as virtuous, brave, enlightened, or uniquely honest. | Moral-self-display error. **Form:** I am morally serious/pure/brave. Therefore my claim should be accepted. | “Unlike all of you compromised people, I alone have the courage to tell the truth.” The speaker’s self-description does not prove the claim. | A person’s integrity may matter in testimony. But self-presented virtue cannot replace evidence. |
| E-23 Spiritual Fallacy | This fallacy uses spiritual language to avoid ordinary standards of evidence, consistency, or accountability. The claim is protected by being moved into a special realm where criticism is treated as spiritual blindness. | Spiritual-exemption error. **Form:** X is spiritually true. Therefore ordinary evidence or criticism does not apply. | “The healer’s failures only show that the patient’s energy resisted transformation.” The explanation makes the claim immune to testing. | Spiritual language can be meaningful in religious or personal contexts. It becomes fallacious when used to make public factual or causal claims untestable. |
| E-24 Appeal to Intuition | This fallacy treats a gut feeling as proof. Intuition may be an important signal, but it can also reflect habit, bias, fear, limited experience, or social conditioning. | Intuition-as-evidence error. **Form:** X feels true. Therefore X is true. | “I cannot explain it, but I just feel the defendant is guilty.” In law, intuition cannot replace evidence. | Expert intuition can be valuable when formed through long experience and feedback. Even then, it remains defeasible and should be checked when stakes are high. |

## Induction, Sampling, Generalization, and Attention-Bias Errors

These fallacies belong together because they arise when an argument moves from **some observed cases** to **a broader conclusion**. Inductive reasoning is not defective in itself. It is indispensable in science, public policy, everyday judgment, risk assessment, and historical interpretation. Deductive validity differs from inductive strength: a deductively valid argument makes it impossible for the premises to be true and the conclusion false, while an inductively strong argument only makes the conclusion probable. Inductive arguments therefore require attention to sample size, sample selection, representativeness, missing cases, statistical terms, defeaters, and the strength of the conclusion.

The shared error in this group is **overextension**: too few cases, biased cases, vivid cases, visible cases, surviving cases, emotionally salient cases, or selectively chosen cases are made to stand for a wider population. Generalizations come in different strengths: strict universal generalizations, statistical generalizations, and defeasible presumptive generalizations do not have the same logical force.

| Fallacy                                                           | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       | Mechanism                                                                                                                               | Example                                                                                                                                                                                              | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                       |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| I-01 `Hasty Generalization` / Faulty Generalization / Argument from Small Numbers / Insufficient Sample | This fallacy draws a broad conclusion from too few cases. The error is not that small samples are always useless, but that the conclusion outruns what the sample can support. The smoking example captures the structure: one person’s long life despite heavy smoking does not defeat large-scale evidence about smoking risk. | Small-sample overextension. **Form:** Sample S is small. S has feature F. Therefore population P has feature F. | “My grandfather smoked heavily and lived to 97, so smoking is not dangerous.” The individual case is memorable, but it cannot outweigh broad medical data. *Case from the outline: Because one risky choice once turned out badly under old conditions, a speaker concludes that the same choice will always turn out badly, even after the circumstances, safeguards, and alternatives have changed.* | If no larger evidence is available and a decision must be made, a small sample may be better than nothing. But the conclusion should remain tentative and proportionate. |
| I-02 `Anecdotal Evidence` / Personal Experience Fallacy | This fallacy uses personal experience, isolated stories, or vivid individual cases as if they were stronger than systematic evidence. Anecdotes are psychologically persuasive because they are concrete, but they often fail to represent the population. | Anecdote-as-data error. **Form:** I experienced or heard case X. Therefore X is generally true. | “My friend recovered after taking this supplement, so the supplement works.” The recovery may be real, but it does not show causation, typicality, or comparative effect. *Case from the outline: Someone gave all wages to family members, later lost savings through that arrangement, and concludes that everyone should never entrust money to relatives. The story is a serious warning, but one household cannot by itself establish a universal rule.* | Anecdotes can generate hypotheses, reveal overlooked cases, or humanize data. They become fallacious when used to replace representative evidence. |
| I-03 `Biased Sample Fallacy` / Unrepresentative Sample | This fallacy draws a conclusion about a population from a sample systematically tilted toward one subgroup. It should be distinguished from hasty generalization: the problem is not only small size, but biased selection. The church-interview example shows the error: interviewing churchgoers to infer what people “everywhere” believe is structurally biased. | Selection-bias error. **Form:** Sample S is drawn from biased subset B of population P. Conclusion about P is drawn from S. | “Nine out of ten people I asked support luxury-car ownership; therefore most people are wealthy.” If the survey was conducted in a Beverly Hills homeowners’ club, the sample is not representative. | A sample may intentionally focus on a subgroup if the conclusion is only about that subgroup. Bias appears when the sample is presented as representing a wider population. |
| I-04 Insufficient Statistics / Statistics of Small Numbers | This fallacy gives a statistical-looking claim from too little data. It differs from ordinary anecdote because it has the appearance of quantitative support: percentages, ratios, or “four out of five” claims can hide tiny sample sizes. | Thin-statistics error. **Form:** n is too small. Percentage or ratio R is calculated. R is treated as stable. | “Four out of five dentists recommend this toothpaste.” If only five dentists were asked, the ratio is too unstable to support the advertisement. | Small-n statistics can be useful in exploratory work, but should be labeled as preliminary. They become fallacious when presented as robust population evidence. |
| I-05 `Cherry Picking` / Selective Evidence / Suppressed Evidence | Cherry picking selects evidence favorable to one’s conclusion while ignoring available evidence that weakens or complicates it. The fallacy is not simply choosing examples; it is choosing examples in a way that misrepresents the total evidential picture. | Selective-evidence error. **Form:** Evidence E1, E2, E3 support X. Contrary evidence C1, C2, C3 is ignored. Therefore X is presented as proven. | A company cites three positive customer reviews and hides thousands of refund requests and safety complaints. The selected evidence is real, but the evidential picture is false. *Case from the outline: A commentator lists only the few cases where a policy helped, while omitting failed cases, costs, and people harmed by the same policy. The selected evidence is real, but the evidential picture is not.* | Focused evidence is acceptable when one is making a narrow point. It becomes cherry picking when relevant contrary or qualifying evidence is omitted. |
| I-06 Selective Attention | This fallacy occurs when the observer notices only cases that fit an expectation and fails to notice cases that do not. It is close to cherry picking, but often less deliberate: attention itself is biased before evidence is even collected. | Attention-filter error. **Form:** Notice cases confirming X. Ignore or fail to notice non-X cases. Conclude X is common or true. | “Every time I think of someone, they text me. This proves mental connection.” The speaker notices hits and forgets the many times thinking of someone was followed by nothing. | Attention is always limited. The fallacy appears when unnoticed cases are relevant and would alter the conclusion if counted. |
| I-07 Misleading Vividness | This fallacy gives disproportionate weight to a vivid, dramatic, emotionally memorable case. The vividness makes the case feel representative even when it is rare or atypical. | Vivid-case overweighing. **Form:** Case C is vivid and memorable. Therefore C is treated as typical or decisive. | After seeing one dramatic airplane accident video, someone concludes that flying is more dangerous than driving, ignoring large-scale accident rates. | Vivid cases can reveal risks that dry statistics hide. They become misleading when vividness replaces frequency, probability, or representativeness. |
| I-08 `Spotlight Fallacy` | This fallacy assumes that what receives public attention is representative of reality. Media coverage, social media virality, scandal, and algorithmic amplification can make rare cases seem common. | Attention-as-frequency error. **Form:** X is frequently seen in media. Therefore X is frequent in the population. | “Every day I see videos of violent subway incidents; therefore the subway is generally unsafe.” The visible feed may not represent the actual incident rate. | Public attention can indicate social concern or newsworthiness. It does not automatically measure frequency. Reliable rates require denominator data. |
| I-09 `Survivorship Fallacy` / Survivorship Bias | This fallacy draws conclusions from visible winners or survivors while ignoring those who failed, disappeared, died, dropped out, or were not able to report. It can be summarized by the idea that “dead men don’t tell tales,” and gives examples in which only survivors’ testimony is available. | Missing-loser error. **Form:** Winners/survivors S are visible. Losers/non-survivors L are invisible. Conclusion is based on S alone. | “All successful founders I interviewed dropped out of college, so dropping out is a good path to success.” The argument ignores the many dropouts who did not succeed and are not being interviewed. | Studying survivors can reveal useful patterns, but only if compared with non-survivors or a suitable baseline. Otherwise it cannot show what caused success. |
| I-10 `Texas Sharpshooter Fallacy` | This fallacy finds a pattern after looking at the data and then treats that pattern as if it confirmed a prior hypothesis. The classic image is shooting at a wall first, then painting the target around the bullet cluster. | After-the-fact pattern selection. **Form:** Data are examined. A cluster or pattern is found. Hypothesis H is drawn around it. H is treated as confirmed. | A company searches hundreds of correlations and finds that customers born in March buy more premium plans; it then claims March birth causes financial ambition. The pattern may be random. *Case from the outline: A critic first decides that a song secretly praises a political figure, then circles only the lyrics, images, and release details that can be made to fit that reading. The target is painted after the shots are fired.* | Exploratory pattern-finding is legitimate if labeled exploratory and tested on new data. It becomes fallacious when post hoc discovery is presented as prior confirmation. |
| I-11 Post-Designation | This fallacy labels a pattern only after observing outcomes, then treats the label as if it had predictive or explanatory power. It is closely related to the Texas sharpshooter fallacy and to after-the-fact category construction. | Retrospective-label error. **Form:** Outcome O occurs. Group/category G is defined after O. G is treated as explanatory. | After several stocks rise, an analyst invents a category called “resilient innovation stocks” and claims the category explains why they rose. | Retrospective categories can be useful for description. They become fallacious when treated as independently predictive without out-of-sample testing. |
| I-12 `Nutpicking Fallacy` | Nutpicking selects the most irrational, embarrassing, or extreme members of a group and presents them as representative of the whole group. It overlaps with ad hominem and overgeneralization, but its statistical error is selection of atypical cases. Nutpicking differs from cherry picking: it specifically chooses “nuts” to represent the opposition. | Extreme-case representativeness error. **Form:** Fringe member F of group G says/does absurd X. Therefore G is absurd. | “Look at this insane comment by one supporter of the movement; this proves the movement is irrational.” The selected person may be marginal. | If the extreme members are numerous, influential, or institutionally endorsed, they may be relevant. The fallacy lies in treating fringe examples as typical without evidence. |
| I-13 Overextended Outrage as Sampling Error | This is the sampling variant of overextended outrage. One or a few vivid bad cases are treated as if they reveal a group-level pattern, usually to generate anger toward the whole group. | Rare-case generalization. **Form:** Person P in group G did bad thing B. Therefore G is generally like B. | A news program repeatedly shows one student cheating and implies that students today are morally ruined, without representative evidence. | The relevance version is listed under R-14. Here the defect is representativeness. |
| I-14 `Jumping to Conclusions` | This fallacy reaches a conclusion before the evidence warrants it. It is a general inductive defect: the arguer moves too quickly from incomplete observation to settled judgment. “Jumping to a conclusion” belongs to the broader problem of plausible but defeasible inference. | Premature-inference error. **Form:** Some evidence E suggests X. Speaker concludes X before alternatives are checked. | “She did not reply to my message for two hours; she must be angry at me.” Many alternatives exist: work, sleep, battery failure, travel, or simple delay. | Practical life often requires provisional judgments. The fallacy appears when a tentative hypothesis is treated as a settled conclusion. |
| I-15 `Ecological Fallacy` | The ecological fallacy infers something about individuals from aggregate group data. Group-level averages, rates, or correlations do not automatically describe each member or explain individual cases. | Group-to-individual statistical error. **Form:** Group G has average/rate property P. Individual i belongs to G. Therefore i has P. | “This neighborhood has a high average income; therefore this tenant must be rich.” The group statistic does not determine the individual’s condition. | Aggregate data can inform probability or context. It cannot replace individual-level evidence when judging an individual. |
| I-16 Reverse Ecological Error / Individual-to-Group Fallacy | This error moves in the opposite direction: it infers a group-level pattern from an individual case. It treats one person’s experience as if it revealed the distribution of the whole population. | Individual-to-group overreach. **Form:** Individual i in group G has property P. Therefore G generally has P. | “My international classmate found the course easy; therefore international students do not struggle with language in this program.” *Case from the outline: After someone reports falling on a dark road and warns others, a commenter says, “I have walked there for ten years and never fallen.” One person’s non-injury is treated as proof that the general risk is unreal.* | Individual cases can raise hypotheses about group patterns. They do not establish prevalence without broader data. |
| I-17 Overgeneralized Rule Application | This is the induction variant of the accident fallacy. A general rule is treated as more universal than the observed range supports, so exceptional circumstances are ignored. | General-rule overapplication. **Form:** Generally, A implies B. Case C is A. Therefore B, ignoring exception E. | “People should not enter restricted areas. A paramedic entered a restricted area to save an injured child. Therefore the paramedic did wrong.” The emergency changes the rule’s application. | The rule-application version is listed under P-14. Here the focus is overgeneralizing the rule’s scope. |
| I-18 Converse Accident / Hasty Induction from Exceptional Case | This fallacy takes an exceptional case and uses it to revise the general rule too broadly. Where accident overapplies a rule to an exception, converse accident overgeneralizes from the exception. | Exception-to-rule overgeneralization. **Form:** Exception E justifies outcome Y in case C. Therefore Y should hold generally. | “A student in a medical emergency should receive a deadline extension. Therefore all students should be able to ignore deadlines.” A special case is generalized into a general rule. | Exceptions can reveal that a rule needs revision. But the revision must be limited to the relevant feature that made the exception justified. |
| I-19 Tokenism as Sampling Error | Tokenism uses a selected individual from a group as if that person were representative. It was already relevant to identity errors, but in sampling terms its defect is treating one chosen token as population evidence. | Selected-token-as-sample error. **Form:** One member of group G says/supports X. Therefore G supports X or X is good for G. | “One disabled employee says the office design is fine; therefore disabled employees have no accessibility problem here.” One testimony cannot settle the group-level issue. | Individual testimony may be highly important, especially for lived experience. It becomes fallacious when treated as exhaustive or representative without support. |
| I-20 Stereotyping as Overgeneralization | Stereotyping assigns a trait to an individual or subgroup because of an overgeneralized image of a broader group. As an induction error, it converts a loose group impression into a firm conclusion about particular people. | Group-image overgeneralization. **Form:** Group G is believed to have trait T. Person P belongs to G. Therefore P has T. | “He is from that region, so he must be conservative.” The group image cannot establish the individual’s views. | Statistical background may inform tentative expectations in some low-stakes contexts. Moral, legal, or intellectual judgments require individual evidence. |
| I-21 `Slothful Induction` | Slothful induction is the opposite of hasty generalization: the evidence is already strong enough to support a reasonable general conclusion, but the speaker refuses to draw it or keeps demanding isolated-case treatment. | Under-generalization error. **Form:** Repeated evidence E strongly supports pattern P. Speaker treats each case as unrelated or inconclusive. | After twenty similar safety failures in the same model, a manager says each failure is just an isolated incident and no pattern has been shown. *Case from the outline: After many similar failures occur in the same system, the manager calls each one an isolated accident. The pattern is kept invisible by refusing to let repeated cases count together.* | Caution is legitimate when evidence is weak or confounded. It becomes fallacious when repeated, relevant evidence is refused without a principled reason. |
| I-22 `False Consensus Fallacy` | This fallacy assumes that one’s own beliefs, habits, interests, or standards are shared by most reasonable people. The speaker treats the self as a representative survey and mistakes personal familiarity for social consensus. | Self-sample majority error. **Form:** I believe, know, or do X. Therefore most normal or reasonable people believe, know, or do X. | *Case from the outline: “I do not know this field, so it must be useless.” Or: “This is my common sense, so anyone who does not know it lacks common sense.” The speaker turns private experience into a supposed majority standard.* | A person’s reaction can be evidence about that person or a starting hypothesis about a group. It becomes fallacious when no representative evidence is supplied. |

## Causation, Statistics, Probability, and Data Errors

These fallacies belong together because they mishandle the movement from **data** to **causal, probabilistic, or practical conclusions**. A statistic may be precise but meaningless; a correlation may be real but not causal; a sequence may be temporal but not explanatory; a probability may be mathematically correct but applied to the wrong reference class; a graph may display real numbers but create a false visual impression. These errors are not a rejection of statistics, but a demand for better critical questions: What is the evidence? How was the sample collected? Are the terms clear? Is the causal claim stronger than the evidence allows? Could there be another cause, reverse causation, or a common background factor?

The common error is **over-certainty from incomplete structure**. The arguer sees numbers, patterns, sequence, correlation, averages, percentages, or dramatic effects, and then moves too quickly to a strong conclusion. The soda example makes the boundary clear: a correlation between soda consumption and metabolic syndrome may be important enough to treat as a hypothesis worth investigating, but it should not automatically be treated as an established causal fact without clarifying the causal mechanism and excluding alternative explanations.
