| Fallacy                                      | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       | Mechanism                                                                                                                                     | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                              |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| C-01 `Questionable Cause` / False Cause / Causal Fallacy                           | This fallacy asserts a causal relationship without enough evidence. The error is not merely being wrong about a cause, but treating association, sequence, similarity, or suspicion as if it established causation. It is the broad family under which many causal fallacies fall.                                                                                           | Unsupported causal inference. **Form:** A and B are related in some way. Therefore A causes B.                                                                  | “Students who use this app get higher grades; therefore the app causes higher grades.” Perhaps stronger students are more likely to use the app, or perhaps both app use and grades are caused by motivation.                                     | A causal hypothesis may be reasonable if there is correlation, mechanism, temporal order, and control for alternatives. The fallacy appears when the conclusion is stronger than the causal evidence. |
| C-02 `Post Hoc` / Post Hoc Ergo Propter Hoc / 事后归因                                 | This fallacy infers causation from temporal sequence: because B happened after A, A caused B. Time order is necessary for many causal claims, but not sufficient. This treats post hoc reasoning as a common causal error that requires critical questions about other possible causes and mechanisms.                                                                     | Temporal-sequence causal error. **Form:** A happened before B. Therefore A caused B.                                                                            | “After the new logo launched, sales increased; therefore the logo caused the increase.” The increase may come from seasonal demand, price changes, advertising, or competitor failure.                                                            | Temporal order is relevant. It can start causal inquiry, but cannot complete it without mechanism and alternative-cause analysis.                                                                     |
| C-03 Strong Imposed Causation / `强置因果` | This fallacy imposes a causal relation merely because two events coexist in space, occur close in time, or appear together in observation. It is broader than post hoc because the relation may be simultaneous rather than sequential. | Co-presence-as-causation error. **Form:** A and B occur together or near each other. Therefore A causes B. | “Crime rates are high in districts with more streetlights; therefore streetlights cause crime.” More likely, streetlights are installed in areas that already have higher nighttime activity or crime risk. *Case from the outline: A commenter connects a title, a color choice, and a release date into one hidden political message, without independent evidence that the maker intended such a link. The causal and symbolic connections are imposed first and justified later.* | Co-presence can be evidence worth investigating. It becomes fallacious when coincidence or co-location is treated as causal proof. |
| C-04 `Correlation-Causation Confusion` | This fallacy treats correlation as proof of causation. A correlation may suggest a possible causal connection, but it may also result from reverse causation, a third variable, selection effects, or coincidence. The arms-spending and infant-mortality example shows the difficulty: impressive statistical correlation still may not establish “what causes what.” | Correlation-to-causation error. **Form:** A and B are correlated. Therefore A causes B. | “Countries with more chocolate consumption have more Nobel Prize winners; therefore chocolate causes intellectual achievement.” Wealth, education, research institutions, and reporting conditions are plausible background variables. *Case from the outline: A speaker observes that many successful people wake early and concludes that waking early causes success. The argument ignores selection effects, work type, health, social resources, and reverse causation.* | Correlation is often the beginning of causal analysis. It becomes causal evidence only when strengthened by mechanism, temporal order, controls, and replication. |
| C-05 Reverse Causation / Retrogressive Causation                                   | This fallacy gets causal direction wrong. A may be correlated with B, but B may cause A rather than A causing B. The infant-mortality and military-spending case raises this critical question: perhaps infant mortality causes military spending, or both are related to deeper conditions.                                                                 | Causal-direction error. **Form:** A is correlated with B. Speaker claims A causes B, though B may cause A.                                                      | “People who visit doctors are sicker, so doctor visits make people sick.” More likely, sickness causes doctor visits.                                                                                                                             | Reverse causation does not always defeat a causal claim; it is one alternative to check. Some causal relations are reciprocal. The fallacy is assuming direction without evidence.                    |
| C-06 Common-Cause Neglect                                                          | This fallacy ignores the possibility that A and B are both caused by C. It is one of the most common ways correlation becomes misleading.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    | Omitted common-cause error. **Form:** A and B occur together. Therefore A causes B, ignoring C.                                                                 | “Students who buy many textbooks get better grades, so buying books causes better grades.” A third factor, academic motivation, may cause both buying books and studying effectively.                                                             | If common causes are controlled for and the association remains, causal inference becomes stronger. The fallacy lies in not asking the common-cause question.                                         |
| C-07 `Oversimplified Cause` / Causal Oversimplification | This fallacy reduces a complex outcome to one cause when several interacting factors are involved. The selected cause may be real, but it is treated as the whole explanation. | Single-cause reduction error. **Form:** Complex outcome Y has causes A, B, C, D. Speaker says A caused Y. | “The company failed because of bad marketing.” Marketing may matter, but product quality, cash flow, logistics, competition, management, and market timing may also matter. *Case from the outline: A complex failure is explained by saying “the subordinate was lazy,” while incentives, instructions, resources, deadlines, and management decisions are ignored. A convenient single cause is made to replace a causal field.* | Simplification is sometimes necessary. It becomes fallacious when relevant causal complexity is erased, especially to assign blame or sell an easy solution. |
| C-08 Causal Reductionism                                                           | This fallacy explains a phenomenon exclusively at one causal level while ignoring other relevant levels. It often reduces social, psychological, institutional, historical, or moral phenomena to a single biological, economic, neurological, or ideological cause.                                                                                                         | One-level explanation error. **Form:** Y is entirely nothing but cause-level A.                                                                                 | “Political polarization is just brain chemistry.” Neurobiology may contribute, but media systems, institutions, incentives, identity, and history also matter.                                                                                    | Reduction can be legitimate when a lower-level explanation really explains the phenomenon for the purpose at hand. The fallacy lies in excluding other relevant causal levels without justification.  |
| C-09 Insignificant Cause                                                           | This fallacy identifies a real cause but one too small to explain the effect. The cause exists, yet its explanatory weight is exaggerated.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Tiny-cause overstatement. **Form:** A contributes slightly to B. Therefore A explains B.                                                                        | “One spelling mistake caused the company’s stock decline.” The mistake may have harmed image slightly, but macroeconomic news or earnings data may explain the decline.                                                                           | Small causes can matter in sensitive systems. The question is proportionality: does the cause have enough weight to explain the effect claimed?                                                       |
| C-10 False Effect                                                                  | This fallacy reasons from a supposed effect to a conclusion about the cause when the causal claim itself has not been established. It should be distinguished from false cause: the problem is drawing conclusions about whether the cause is right or wrong based on an effect that is false or uncertain.                                                                    | Effect-based causal misfire. **Form:** X supposedly causes Y. Y is false/right/wrong. Therefore X is false/right/wrong.                                         | “People used to claim sitting close to the TV makes you blind. That is false; therefore there is no reason not to sit close.” The false effect does not prove there are no other adverse effects.                                                 | If the only claim under discussion is the specific causal claim “X causes Y,” showing Y is false or not caused by X can be relevant. The fallacy appears when that limited point is overextended.     |
| C-11 `Regression Fallacy`                                                            | This fallacy mistakes regression toward the mean for a causal effect. Extreme outcomes are often followed by less extreme outcomes simply because random variation moves back toward average.                                                                                                                                                                                | Regression-to-mean error. **Form:** Extreme result E occurs. Intervention A follows. Later result is less extreme. Therefore A caused improvement.              | A student performs unusually badly on one test, receives a harsh lecture, then scores closer to average next time. The improvement may be ordinary regression, not the lecture’s effect.                                                          | Interventions can work. To show this, compare with controls, repeated measures, or expected regression.                                                                                               |
| C-12 Appeal to Coincidence                                                         | This fallacy dismisses a pattern as “just coincidence” when the pattern may require explanation. It is the reverse danger of overcausal thinking: instead of inventing causation, it refuses to consider causation.                                                                                                                                                          | Coincidence-as-dismissal error. **Form:** A and B align repeatedly. Speaker says it is coincidence without examining probability or mechanism.                  | A manager’s relatives repeatedly win internal contracts. When questioned, he says, “It is just coincidence.” Repetition may require scrutiny.                                                                                                     | Coincidences do happen. The fallacy appears when coincidence is used to avoid investigation despite pattern strength, prior probability, or conflict of interest.                                     |
| C-13 Magical Thinking / Superstitious Thinking | Magical thinking creates causal or correlational links based on superstition, symbolic association, or perceived cosmic balance rather than evidence. Examples include prayer for rain followed by rain, or avoiding the 13th floor because of bad luck. | Superstitious-causal error. **Form:** A happens. B happens. A is magically connected to B. | “I wore this shirt during the last successful exam; therefore it brings good grades.” The shirt may be psychologically comforting, but it has not been shown to cause performance. *Case from the outline: After a project fails, the team says the timing was unlucky and turns to rituals for better fortune next time, instead of examining design, planning, incentives, or execution.* | Rituals may help confidence or focus. They become fallacious when treated as external causal mechanisms without evidence. |
| C-14 Homunculus Fallacy                                                            | This fallacy explains a process by positing a smaller agent inside it that performs the very process being explained. It merely relocates the mystery instead of explaining it.                                                                                                                                                                                              | Internal-agent pseudo-explanation. **Form:** System S does X because an inner agent H inside S does X.                                                          | “The brain understands language because there is a little interpreter inside the brain reading the words.” This repeats the problem: how does the interpreter understand?                                                                         | Simplified internal models can be pedagogically useful if not taken literally. The fallacy appears when the internal agent is treated as an explanation rather than a metaphor.                       |
| C-15 Anthropomorphism                                                              | Anthropomorphism attributes human intentions, emotions, or reasoning to non-human entities where such attribution is unsupported. It can distort causal explanation by replacing mechanism with imagined agency.                                                                                                                                                             | Human-intention projection. **Form:** Non-human system N behaves in way B. Therefore N wanted, planned, or felt X.                                              | “The market punished us because it hates uncertainty.” Markets do not literally hate; the explanation should refer to investor behavior, liquidity, expectations, or risk.                                                                        | Anthropomorphic language can be useful metaphor. It becomes fallacious when metaphor is mistaken for causal explanation.                                                                              |
| C-16 Quantum Physics Fallacy                                                       | This fallacy invokes quantum physics to support claims that have no demonstrated connection to quantum theory. Scientific prestige and mystery are used to justify vague causal claims.                                                                                                                                                                                      | Misused-science causal mystification. **Form:** Quantum physics is strange. X is strange. Therefore quantum physics supports X.                                 | “Quantum mechanics proves that your thoughts can manifest wealth.” No actual quantum mechanism connecting thought and wealth is provided.                                                                                                         | Quantum theory is relevant in appropriate physical contexts. The fallacy lies in using the term as a prestige label for unrelated claims.                                                             |
| C-17 `Base Rate Fallacy`                                                           | This fallacy ignores the background frequency of an event when interpreting specific evidence. The prosecutor’s variation confuses the probability of evidence given guilt with the probability of guilt given evidence.                                                                                                                     | Background-rate neglect. **Form:** Evidence E is likely if H. Therefore H is likely, ignoring how common H is overall.                                          | A medical test is 99% accurate for a rare disease affecting 1 in 10,000 people. A positive result does not automatically mean the patient almost certainly has the disease; false positives may still outnumber true positives.                   | Specific evidence matters, but it must be interpreted against the relevant base rate.                                                                                                                 |
| C-18 `Prosecutor’s Fallacy`                                                          | This fallacy confuses the probability of evidence if the defendant is innocent with the probability that the defendant is innocent given the evidence. It is a conditional-probability error. It can be described as treating P(A given B) as if it were P(B given A).                                                                                                      | Conditional-probability reversal. **Form:** P(Evidence given Innocent) is low. Therefore P(Innocent given Evidence) is low.                                             | “Only 1 in a million people would match this DNA profile; therefore there is only a 1 in a million chance the defendant is innocent.” The relevant calculation must include population size, testing procedure, lab error, and prior probability. | Statistical evidence can be powerful in court. It must be interpreted with the correct conditional probability and reference class.                                                                   |
| C-19 `Gambler’s Fallacy`                                                           | This fallacy assumes that independent random events are self-correcting in the short run. After many reds, black feels “due”; after many losses, a win feels due. The error is treating independent trials as if they remember previous outcomes.                                                                                                                            | Independence misunderstanding. **Form:** Random event A has occurred many times. Therefore opposite event B is now more likely.                                 | A roulette wheel lands red six times in a row. Greg believes black is now “due,” and bets heavily. The wheel has no memory.                                                                                                                       | In non-independent systems, past events can affect future probabilities. The fallacy concerns independent trials being treated as dependent.                                                          |
| C-20 Hot Hand Fallacy                                                              | This fallacy is often presented as the reverse of the gambler’s fallacy: because someone has succeeded several times in a row, success is assumed to be more likely next time. The error is seeing a streak as causal evidence without checking independence, skill, context, or randomness.                                                                                 | Streak-overinterpretation error. **Form:** A succeeded repeatedly. Therefore A is more likely to succeed again.                                                 | “This trader made five profitable trades in a row, so the next trade is very likely to win.” The streak may be luck or market conditions.                                                                                                         | Some streaks reflect skill, learning, momentum, or changing conditions. The fallacy appears when streak alone is treated as proof.                                                                    |
| C-21 Ludic Fallacy                                                                 | This fallacy treats real-world uncertainty as if it behaved like a clean game of chance with known rules, fixed probabilities, and closed possibilities. The probability example around a gynecologist’s office door shows the danger of applying abstract statistics while ignoring real context.                                                                  | Game-model misuse. **Form:** Real-world situation S is treated like simplified game G. Therefore probabilities from G are applied to S.                         | “Since about half the population is female, the chance that the next patient from this clinic is female is 50%.” If the door is to a gynecologist’s office, the reference class changes.                                                          | Models are useful simplifications. The fallacy lies in forgetting that the real situation may not match the model’s assumptions.                                                                      |
| C-22 `Lying with Statistics` / Statistical Fallacies / Curve-Fitting / Overfitting | This is a family of fallacies in which data are collected, selected, graphed, or interpreted in a biased way. Deceptive statistics often involve manipulating presentation, such as graph scales, loose definitions, and selective counting.                                                                                                              | Statistical-presentation distortion. **Form:** Statistic S is manipulated or presented selectively to support claim X.                                          | A graph shows a “huge spike” from 20% to 22% by using a vertical axis from 20% to 25%, making a small change look dramatic.                                                                                                                       | Statistics are not deceptive by nature. Careful definitions, transparent methods, full denominators, and honest visualization can make statistical argument strong.                                   |
| C-23 Fake Precision / False Precision                                              | This fallacy uses exact numbers where the underlying concept, measurement, or data source is too vague to justify precision. Meaningless statistics create the same problem: a precise percentage can be meaningless when key terms are vague or unknowable.                                                                                           | Precision-without-measurement error. **Form:** Vague phenomenon V is assigned exact figure N%.                                                                  | “90% of major manipulators would disappear if ordinary citizens resisted corruption.” If “major manipulator” is undefined and hard to measure, the exact percentage creates false authority.                                                      | Precision is valuable when measurement is clear. It is fallacious when exactness hides vagueness, uncertainty, or lack of access to data.                                                             |
| C-24 McNamara Fallacy / Quantitative Fallacy | This fallacy bases a decision only on what can be easily measured, ignores what cannot be easily measured, and then treats the unmeasured as unimportant or nonexistent. The logical form is: measure what is easy, disregard what is not, then presume the unmeasured does not matter. | Measurable-only decision error. **Form:** Only measurable factors M are counted. Non-measurable factors Q are ignored. Decision is made as if Q does not exist. | A university evaluates teaching only by number of slides, attendance, and test scores, ignoring intellectual growth, writing quality, mentorship, and student confidence. *Case from the outline: A relationship is judged by money spent: the more expensive the gifts, the greater the love. A measurable input is treated as the whole reality of care, respect, reliability, and mutual attention.* | Quantitative metrics are useful. The fallacy appears when measurement convenience is mistaken for importance. |
| C-25 Multiple Comparisons Fallacy                                                  | This fallacy tests many relationships and treats one statistically significant result as meaningful without adjusting for the number of tests. The more comparisons are made, the more likely some pattern appears by chance.                                                                                                                                                | Many-tests false-positive error. **Form:** Test many hypotheses. Find one significant result. Treat it as real without correction or replication.               | A researcher tests 200 foods against 50 symptoms and finds that blueberries correlate with fewer headaches on Tuesdays. Without correction and replication, the pattern may be noise.                                                             | Multiple testing is legitimate when corrected, pre-registered, or followed by independent replication.                                                                                                |
| C-26 Broken Window Fallacy                                                         | This fallacy focuses on visible economic activity after destruction while ignoring unseen opportunity costs. The repair spending is counted as benefit, while the alternative use of resources is neglected.                                                                                                                                                                 | Visible-benefit / hidden-cost error. **Form:** Destruction creates spending. Spending is good. Therefore destruction benefits the economy.                      | “The storm helped the economy because it created construction jobs.” The money spent rebuilding could have been used for new goods, savings, education, or investment.                                                                            | Disaster recovery can generate employment, but that does not mean destruction is economically beneficial overall. One must count opportunity costs.                                                   |
| C-27 `Average Misuse` / 平均值误用                                                      | This fallacy draws a conclusion from an average while ignoring distribution, variance, extremes, or relevant subgroups. The average may be mathematically correct but practically misleading.                                                                                                                                                                                | Average-as-uniformity error. **Form:** Average of group G is A. Therefore each relevant case is near A or safe under A.                                         | “This river is on average only 0.8 meters deep, so a 1.8-meter person cannot drown.” The average hides deep points, currents, and local variation.                                                                                                | Averages are useful summaries. They become misleading when the conclusion depends on distribution rather than central tendency.                                                                       |
| C-28 Non-Comparable Data / 数据不可比                                                   | This fallacy compares two numbers that do not share the same definition, denominator, measurement method, time frame, population, or context. The numerical comparison looks objective but lacks a common basis.                                                                                                                                                             | Invalid-comparison error. **Form:** Data A and B are compared, though their units, methods, or populations differ.                                              | “Country A has fewer reported workplace injuries than Country B, so Country A is safer.” If Country A underreports injuries or uses a narrower definition, the data are not comparable.                                                           | Data can be compared after normalization, standardization, and methodological checking. The fallacy appears when raw numbers are compared without a shared basis.                                     |
| C-29 Unknowable Statistics                                                         | This error uses statistical claims about things that cannot realistically be counted or verified. It is close to fake precision but emphasizes access: even if terms were clear, the data may be practically unavailable. Meaningless and unknowable statistics are major errors in statistical reasoning.                                                       | Unverifiable-statistic error. **Form:** X% of hidden/unobservable group G has property P.                                                                       | “73% of people secretly regret their marriage.” Unless there is a method for accessing sincere private regret, the exact figure is not credible.                                                                                                  | Hard-to-measure topics can be studied through careful surveys and indirect methods. The fallacy lies in presenting unknowable numbers as if directly measured.                                        |

## Language, Semantics, Definition, and Context Errors

These fallacies belong together because they exploit the instability of **natural language**. Words can be ambiguous, vague, emotionally loaded, context-dependent, metaphorical, technical, quoted selectively, or defined in a way that smuggles in the desired conclusion. Natural-language argumentation is directly relevant here: ambiguity, vagueness, loaded terms, question-begging language, equivocation, amphiboly, analogy, and slippery slope must be evaluated in the context of dialogue, not as isolated dictionary puzzles.

The common error is **meaning drift**. A term appears stable, but its sense, scope, standard, emotional force, or context changes as the argument proceeds. Realistic equivocations may not be obvious in short examples; in long arguments, the same word may occur in different premises under different standards of precision, so the critic must inspect the whole argument and its dialogue context.  Ambiguity, type-token confusion, the continuum fallacy, etymological fallacy, contextomy, definist fallacy, and related errors are distinct but connected failures of language use.

| Fallacy                                                            | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       | Mechanism                                                                                                                                          | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                     |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| L-01 `Ambiguity Fallacy` / Ambiguous Assertion / Amphibology / Semantical Ambiguity | This fallacy uses an unclear expression with more than one possible meaning, then draws a conclusion that depends on one meaning while the premise only supports another. It can be defined as an unclear phrase with multiple definitions that fails to support the conclusion. | Ambiguous-meaning error. **Form:** Claim X uses ambiguous term T. Conclusion Y depends on one interpretation of T, while the premise supports another or weaker one. | “We have a good understanding of the universe; therefore, we know exactly how it began.” “Good understanding” may mean broad scientific knowledge, not complete knowledge of every origin question. | Ambiguity is common and not automatically fallacious. It becomes fallacious when the conclusion depends on a meaning that has not been established. |
| L-02 `Equivocation` / 偷换概念 | Equivocation occurs when the same word or phrase shifts meaning inside an argument. Each premise may look plausible separately, but they can be plausible only under different meanings of the key term. The legal-obligation example shows the pattern: “obligatory” may shift from legally obligatory to morally obligatory. | Same-word meaning-shift. **Form:** T means T1 in premise 1. T means T2 in premise 2. Conclusion treats T1 and T2 as the same. | “Following the law is obligatory. Failing to do what is obligatory is morally wrong. Therefore, failing to follow the law is morally wrong.” The word “obligatory” shifts from legal obligation to moral obligation. | A stipulated definition can remove ambiguity if it is openly stated and accepted for the argument. But unilateral redefinition may itself become loaded or question-begging. |
| L-03 Amphiboly / Syntactic Ambiguity | Amphiboly arises from ambiguous grammar or sentence structure rather than from a single ambiguous word. The words may be clear individually, but the structure allows more than one reading. | Syntax-based ambiguity. **Form:** Sentence S can grammatically mean A or B. Argument depends on one reading while presenting another. | “The professor said after the exam the students would celebrate.” Did the professor say it after the exam, or will the students celebrate after the exam? A conclusion based on the wrong structure can mislead. | Ambiguous grammar is often accidental. It becomes fallacious when a conclusion exploits the ambiguity or refuses clarification. |
| L-04 `Accent Fallacy` / Fallacy of Accent / Misleading Emphasis | This fallacy changes the meaning or implication of a statement by shifting emphasis, stress, typography, headline selection, or vocal tone. The same words can suggest different commitments depending on where emphasis falls. | Emphasis-shift error. **Form:** Statement S is presented with emphasis E, producing implication I not present in the original neutral statement. | “I didn’t say he stole the money” can imply different things depending on which word is stressed: maybe someone else said it, maybe he borrowed it, maybe he stole something else. | Emphasis is a normal part of speech. It becomes fallacious when emphasis creates a misleading inference not supported by the original claim. |
| L-05 `Vagueness` | Vagueness occurs when a term has unclear boundaries: it is not obvious where it begins or ends. Vague terms are not automatically bad; they are often necessary in ordinary language. The problem appears when the argument uses unclear boundaries to avoid commitment or force an unwarranted conclusion. | Boundary-indeterminacy error. **Form:** Term T has unclear boundary. Speaker exploits the boundary to avoid decision or force conclusion. | “The policy should apply only to serious misconduct.” If “serious” is never clarified, the rule can be applied or withheld arbitrarily. | Many legal, moral, and social terms are necessarily vague. The issue is whether enough working criteria are provided for the argument’s purpose. |
| L-06 `Argument of the Beard` / Continuum Fallacy / Sorites Fallacy / Heap Fallacy / Line-Drawing Fallacy | This fallacy argues that because there is no precise cut-off point between two extremes, no useful distinction exists. The classic beard / heap structure is: if one hair does not make a beard, then no number of single-hair changes will matter.  Vagueness also connects to slippery-slope style reasoning: vague terms can make it hard to know where to stop accepting small steps. | Continuum-boundary error. **Form:** X and Y lie on a continuum. No exact point separates them. Therefore X and Y are not meaningfully different. | “There is no exact moment when a person becomes old, so age categories are meaningless.” The lack of a sharp boundary does not remove all practical distinctions. | Some boundaries are arbitrary but still useful. The fallacy lies in treating lack of perfect precision as lack of any distinction. |
| L-07 `Prejudicial Language` / Loaded Terms | This fallacy uses language that contains a negative or positive judgment before the argument has established it. The term does not merely describe; it pushes the audience toward a conclusion. | Evaluative-wording error. **Form:** X is described with loaded term T. T already contains the desired evaluation. | “The reformers are trying to smuggle radical ideology into schools.” The word “smuggle” implies secrecy and wrongdoing before those facts are shown. | Evaluative language can be accurate after evidence supports it. It becomes fallacious when it performs the evaluation before the argument. |
| L-08 Question-Begging Language | This fallacy states a disputed conclusion inside apparently descriptive language. It is close to loaded terms but more structurally tied to the conclusion: the wording already assumes the point at issue. “Loaded terms and question-begging language” are a major part of natural language argumentation. | Conclusion-smuggled-in-language error. **Form:** Disputed claim X is built into the wording of premise P. | “The victim of censorship was prevented from publishing.” If the dispute is whether the restriction counts as censorship, the wording has already assumed the conclusion. | Sometimes the label is already established by context or evidence. The fallacy appears when the label is used before the contested classification is argued. |
| L-09 `Persuasive Definition` | A persuasive definition gives a term an emotionally or evaluatively charged definition while appearing to clarify meaning. It redefines the word so that one side wins by definition. | Definition-as-persuasion error. **Form:** Define X in value-laden way D. Conclude X should be accepted or rejected. | “Freedom means never having to follow government rules; therefore all regulation is anti-freedom.” The definition is doing the argumentative work. | A proposed definition can be openly defended. It becomes fallacious when presented as neutral while secretly settling the dispute. |
| L-10 `Definist Fallacy` | The definist fallacy defines a term in a way that embeds a controversial moral, political, or metaphysical claim. It is more aggressive than ordinary stipulation because it treats a disputed theory as part of meaning. | Controversial theory built into definition. **Form:** X is defined as D, where D contains contested claim C. Therefore C is treated as settled. | “Real education is the liberation of the student from all institutions; therefore schools cannot provide real education.” The anti-institutional conclusion has been inserted into the definition. | Technical terms may be theory-laden. The problem is pretending a controversial theoretical definition is ordinary neutral usage. |
| L-11 Definitional Retreat | This fallacy retreats to a narrower or altered definition after a claim has been challenged. The speaker begins with a broad claim, then protects it by changing what the key term means. | Retreating-definition error. **Form:** Claim about T is challenged. Speaker redefines T more narrowly so the claim survives. | “All successful people are disciplined.” Counterexample: a successful but chaotic artist. “Well, by successful I mean internally fulfilled, not publicly successful.” The original claim has shifted. | Clarifying a term after misunderstanding is legitimate. It becomes fallacious when the clarification is introduced only to avoid counterexample or defeat. |
| L-12 Appeal to Definition | This fallacy treats a dictionary definition as if it settled a substantive argument. Dictionaries record usage; they do not by themselves decide legal, ethical, scientific, or political disputes. | Dictionary-as-decision error. **Form:** Dictionary defines W as D. Therefore dispute about W is settled. | “The dictionary defines marriage this way, so the law cannot define it otherwise.” A dictionary definition cannot by itself decide institutional design. | Definitions matter, especially in law and technical fields. But when the dispute concerns how a term should be used, definition is part of the argument, not the end of it. |
| L-13 Etymological Fallacy | This fallacy assumes that the original or historical meaning of a word is its true or proper meaning now. Word origins may be informative, but meanings change through use. | Origin-of-word-as-meaning error. **Form:** Word W originally meant O. Therefore W now really means O. | “The word ‘decimate’ originally meant to reduce by one-tenth; therefore using it to mean severe destruction is wrong and any argument using it that way fails.” Contemporary usage may differ. | Etymology can clarify history and nuance. It becomes fallacious when origin is treated as binding current meaning. |
| L-14 If-By-Whiskey | This fallacy uses deliberately balanced, ambiguous language to avoid commitment while appearing to answer. It says one thing to one audience and another thing to another audience. | Strategic equivocation / diplomatic ambiguity. **Form:** If by X you mean X1, I support it; if by X you mean X2, I oppose it; final position remains hidden. | “If by reform you mean protecting families, I support reform; if by reform you mean reckless destruction, I oppose reform.” The speaker may avoid saying whether the actual proposal is supported. | Careful distinction between meanings is legitimate. It becomes fallacious when distinctions are used to avoid taking a position on the concrete issue. |
| L-15 Use-Mention Error | This fallacy confuses using a word to refer to something with mentioning the word as a linguistic item. The object-level and word-level are mixed. | Use/mention confusion. **Form:** A claim about word W is treated as a claim about thing W refers to, or vice versa. | “‘Justice’ has seven letters; therefore justice is linguistically simple.” The first claim concerns the word, not the concept. | Discussing language often requires moving between word and object. The distinction must be kept explicit. |
| L-16 Type-Token Fallacy | This fallacy confuses a type, the general kind, with a token, a particular instance. Examples such as “Toyota manufactures dozens of cars” show the issue: this can mean models, not physical individual vehicles. | Type-token confusion. **Form:** Speaker refers to type T. Listener responds as if token t was meant, or reverse. | “I have the same suit as that actor.” “Do you take turns wearing it?” The first speaker means same model/type, not the identical physical suit. | Type-token shifts are common in casual jokes. They become fallacious when the confusion supports a conclusion. |
| L-17 Reification / Hypostatization | Reification treats an abstraction, model, institution, category, or metaphor as if it were a concrete thing or agent. The error is turning a useful linguistic abstraction into a literal entity. | Abstraction-as-thing error. **Form:** Abstract term A is discussed as if it were concrete object or agent C. | “History wants revenge.” History does not literally want anything; people, institutions, memory, and material conditions produce consequences. | Reification can be useful metaphor. It becomes fallacious when the metaphor is treated as literal explanation. |
| L-18 Contextomy as Language-Context Error / Quoting Out of Context | This is the language-context variant of contextomy. The quoted words are not merely sourced badly; their meaning changes because qualifiers, irony, negation, contrast, or the original question have been removed. | Meaning-by-context removal. **Form:** Source says S in context C. Quoter removes C, so S now appears to support X. | A review says, “The first act is brilliant, though the film collapses afterward.” An advertisement quotes only: “brilliant.” | The evidence-source version is listed under S-11. Here the focus is how context determines meaning. |
| L-19 Weasel Wording | Weasel wording uses vague qualifiers to create a stronger impression than the statement actually warrants. It often appears in marketing, politics, and institutional language. Examples such as “Save up to 50% or more” show how ambiguity conceals the actual claim. | Vague-qualification manipulation. **Form:** Statement uses “up to,” “may,” “could,” “some,” “many,” or “virtually” to imply more than it commits to. | “This supplement may help support immune balance.” The claim sounds medical, but it commits to almost nothing measurable. | Qualifiers are necessary for accuracy. They become manipulative when they create an inflated impression while avoiding responsibility. |
| L-20 Word Magic | This fallacy assumes that because there is a word for something, the thing must exist or have the structure implied by the word. It is debatable as a fallacy, but it captures a common language error. | Word-existence confusion. **Form:** There is a word W. Therefore W names a real entity or natural category. | “There is a word ‘destiny,’ so destiny must be a real force guiding events.” The word records a concept, not proof of an entity. | Words often track real distinctions. But the existence of a term is not evidence that its referent exists. |
| L-21 Fact-to-Fiction Fallacy | This fallacy moves from a fact about language, narrative, classification, or representation to a claim about reality itself, or treats a fictional construction as if it established a real-world fact. | Representation-to-reality error. **Form:** In story/model/category X, P is true. Therefore P is true in reality. | “In this popular crime drama, lie detectors reliably expose criminals; therefore polygraph evidence should be decisive in court.” Fictional representation is not empirical evidence. | Fiction can illustrate possibilities or moral tensions. It becomes fallacious when treated as factual proof. |
| L-22 `混淆概念` / Concept Confusion | This error occurs when related but distinct concepts are treated as the same. It is broader than equivocation: the terms may not be identical, but their conceptual boundaries are blurred. | Concept-boundary confusion. **Form:** Concept A is related to concept B. Speaker treats A and B as interchangeable. | “Legality, morality, and justice are all about what should be done; therefore if something is legal, it is morally just.” Related concepts are collapsed into one. | Concepts can overlap and interact. The fallacy appears when distinctions necessary for the argument are erased. |
| L-23 Too Broad Definition | A definition is too broad when it includes things that should not be included. “Too broad” is mainly a definitional error rather than a standard fallacy, but it often causes bad arguments. | Overinclusive definition. **Form:** X is defined as D. D includes non-X cases. | “A vehicle is anything that moves people.” Then shoes, elevators, and escalators all become vehicles in the same sense, which may not fit the argument. | Broad definitions may be useful for special theoretical purposes. They must be marked as such and not smuggled into ordinary contexts. |
| L-24 Too Narrow Definition | A definition is too narrow when it excludes cases that should be included. Like overbroad definition, this may be a factual or conceptual error but can distort argument. | Underinclusive definition. **Form:** X is defined as D. D excludes genuine X cases. | “A scientist is someone who works in a laboratory.” This excludes field scientists, theoretical physicists, data scientists, and many others. | Narrow technical definitions can be legitimate within a system. The issue is whether the definition fits the argumentative purpose. |
| L-25 Re-labeling / Euphemism and Dysphemism | This error changes the perceived moral or practical status of an act by renaming it. Euphemism softens; dysphemism hardens. The underlying act is not analyzed; its label is changed. | Label-substitution error. **Form:** Act A is renamed with favorable/unfavorable term T. Audience’s evaluation changes without new evidence. | “We are not firing workers; we are optimizing human resources.” Or: “They are not revising policy; they are betraying the public.” | Naming can clarify moral reality. It becomes fallacious when label choice substitutes for analysis of what happened. |

## Analogy, Comparison, Slippery-Slope, and Thought-Experiment Errors

These fallacies belong together because they reason by **similarity, contrast, imagined cases, or chains of consequences**. Analogy and comparison are not inferior forms of reasoning. They are often indispensable: legal reasoning compares cases, moral philosophy uses thought experiments, political argument compares institutions, and ordinary reasoning constantly asks whether one case is relevantly like another. Analogy should not be dismissed as fallacious. He says the key questions are whether the compared cases are similar in the relevant respect, whether important differences have been ignored, and whether a counteranalogy is stronger.

The shared error in this group is **uncontrolled transfer**. A feature, judgment, rule, consequence, or emotional impression is moved from one case to another without showing that the transfer is justified. Slippery-slope arguments add a further structure: once a first step is conceded, a speaker presses for consistency through a chain of allegedly similar steps, sometimes leading to an absurd or disastrous endpoint. such arguments are not always fallacious; the issue is whether the steps are genuinely linked, whether the differences are relevant, and whether the predicted development is merely possible or actually likely.  

| Fallacy                                       | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         | Mechanism                                                                                                                                           | Example                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                                                    |
| ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| M-01 `Weak Analogy` / False Analogy / Faulty Analogy / Bad Analogy / False Metaphor | This fallacy uses an analogy to support a conclusion, but the two cases are not similar in the relevant respect. Analogies are better described as weak or strong rather than simply true or false; the weakness lies in transferring a property from one case to another when the relevant similarities are insufficient. | Irrelevant-similarity transfer. **Form:** X is like Y in respect A. Y has property P. Therefore X has P, though A is not relevant to P. | “Running a country is like running a household; households must cut spending when income falls, so the state must do the same.” The analogy ignores monetary sovereignty, taxation, debt markets, public investment, and macroeconomic feedback. *Case from the outline: A manager says a company is like a family, so employees should show unconditional loyalty and accept personal sacrifice. The analogy transfers family duties into an employment relation without arguing for the relevant similarities.* | Analogies are legitimate when the similarities concern the feature being inferred. A good analogy identifies both likenesses and limits. |
| M-02 Extended Analogy | This fallacy begins with a reasonable analogy but pushes it beyond the point where the similarity still holds. The initial comparison may clarify one aspect, but later conclusions rely on features that the compared cases do not share. | Overextended-transfer error. **Form:** X is like Y in respect A. Speaker keeps transferring B, C, D, though only A was relevant. | “The brain is like a computer” may help explain information processing. But saying “therefore memory files can simply be deleted like computer files” overextends the analogy. | Extended analogy can be useful for teaching if its limits are stated. It becomes fallacious when the metaphor starts generating unsupported conclusions. |
| M-03 Faulty Comparison | Faulty comparison compares two cases without ensuring that they are comparable in relevant dimensions. The problem is broader than weak analogy: the comparison may use different baselines, categories, time frames, purposes, or standards. | Invalid-comparison frame. **Form:** A and B are compared, but the basis of comparison differs. | “This small private college is worse than a national university because it publishes fewer total papers.” The comparison ignores size, mission, funding, and research obligations. | Comparison is valid when the dimensions, denominators, and purposes are aligned. The critic should ask: comparable in what respect, for what conclusion? |
| M-04 Incomplete Comparison | This fallacy makes a comparative claim without specifying the comparison class. Words like “better,” “safer,” “cheaper,” “more efficient,” or “faster” require an object of comparison. | Missing-comparator error. **Form:** X is more P, but more P than what is unspecified. | “This app is more secure.” More secure than the previous version? Its competitors? Doing nothing? Paper records? The claim cannot be evaluated. | In conversation, context may supply the missing comparison. It becomes fallacious in argument or advertising when the missing comparator creates an inflated impression. |
| M-05 `False Equivalence` | False equivalence treats two things as morally, factually, or practically equivalent because they share a surface similarity, while ignoring important differences in scale, intention, evidence, consequence, or context. | Superficial-equality error. **Form:** A and B share feature F. Therefore A and B are equivalent. | “Both candidates made inaccurate statements, so they are equally dishonest.” One may have made one corrected mistake, while the other repeatedly fabricated central claims. | Equivalence can be fair when the relevant features really match. The fallacy appears when equality of one feature is inflated into equality overall. |
| M-06 `False Balance` | False balance presents two sides as equally credible merely because there are two sides. It is common in journalism, classroom debate, and public controversy when balance is confused with proportional evidence. | Symmetry-as-credibility error. **Form:** There is a dispute between A and B. Therefore A and B deserve equal weight. | A news segment gives equal time to climate scientists and a non-expert conspiracy advocate, creating the impression that the evidence is evenly divided. | Fairness may require hearing minority views. But equal airtime is not the same as equal evidential weight. |
| M-07 `Distinction Without a Difference` | This fallacy tries to defend a position by drawing a verbal distinction that does not change the relevant issue. The speaker appears to answer criticism, but the alleged distinction has no practical or conceptual effect. | Empty-distinction error. **Form:** A is criticized. Speaker says it is not A, but B. Yet A and B do not differ in the relevant respect. | “We are not spying on users; we are continuously collecting private behavioral signals without their awareness.” The new label does not change the privacy issue. *Case from the outline: A listener rejects the sleep-as-battery analogy because brains are biological and batteries are hardware. The difference is real, but it does not touch the limited comparison about depletion and recovery.* | Some distinctions initially sound thin but later matter. The fallacy appears only when the distinction fails to affect the argument’s relevant criterion. |
| M-08 `Unwarranted Contrast` / Some-Are-Some-Are-Not Error | This fallacy treats conversational implication as logical implication. The form is: from “some S are P,” one cannot logically infer “some S are not P.” Ordinary language often implies “not all,” but logic does not. | Implicature-as-implication error. **Form:** Some S are P. Therefore some S are not P. Or: some S are not P. Therefore some S are P. | “Some students passed the exam. Therefore some students failed.” This may be pragmatically suggested, but it does not logically follow. It is possible that all students passed. | In ordinary conversation, “some” often conversationally suggests “not all.” In strict reasoning, that implication must be stated or shown. |
| M-09 `Package-Deal Fallacy` | This fallacy treats several distinct positions as an inseparable package. It assumes that accepting one item commits a person to all items in the bundle, or rejecting one item requires rejecting the whole package. | Artificial-bundle error. **Form:** A, B, C, and D are presented as one package. Accepting or rejecting one is treated as accepting or rejecting all. | “If you support environmental regulation, you must also support every specific policy proposed by this party.” Different environmental policies can be accepted or rejected separately. | Some policies or theories do form integrated packages. The fallacy appears when separable claims are bundled for rhetorical control. |
| M-10 `Fallacy of Composition` | This fallacy infers that what is true of the parts must be true of the whole. The error is ignoring emergent structure, interaction, scale, or collective arrangement. | Part-to-whole transfer. **Form:** Each part of X has property P. Therefore X as a whole has property P. | “Every player on this team is individually talented; therefore the team must be tactically excellent.” Coordination, roles, coaching, and chemistry matter. | Some properties do transfer from parts to whole: if every tile is red, the mosaic is red. The issue is whether the property is distributive or collective. |
| M-11 `Fallacy of Division` | This fallacy infers that what is true of the whole must be true of each part. The whole may have a property that no individual part has by itself. | Whole-to-part transfer. **Form:** Whole X has property P. Therefore each part of X has P. | “This university is wealthy, so every student here must be wealthy.” Institutional wealth does not distribute to every member. | Some properties do divide: if a box contains only metal parts, each part is metal. The fallacy appears when a collective property is treated as individual. |
| M-12 Every-and-All as Whole-Part Comparison | This is the comparison variant of the every-and-all error. It compares individual members with the whole group as if distributive, collective, relational, and emergent properties moved in the same way. | Whole-part comparison error. **Form:** Every member is P; therefore the whole is P. Or: the whole is P; therefore each member is P. | Every department saved money this month, so the company’s financial position must have improved. If revenue collapsed or shared costs rose, the whole may not improve. | The formal quantifier version is listed under F-21. Here the focus is comparison between parts and wholes. |
| M-13 `Slippery Slope` | A slippery-slope argument claims that accepting a first step will lead through a chain of further steps to an unacceptable endpoint. The structure is this: once a small difference is conceded as insignificant, the same reasoning may be repeated step by step until an absurd result is reached. | Chain-of-concession error. **Form:** If A0 is accepted, A1 must be accepted; then A2; then A3; eventually unacceptable Ak. Therefore reject A0. | “If we allow students one emergency extension, soon everyone will ignore every deadline and the course will collapse.” The argument assumes each step follows from the previous one. | Slippery-slope reasoning can be reasonable when there are real institutional incentives, weak stopping points, precedent effects, or causal mechanisms. It is fallacious when the chain is merely imagined. |
| M-14 Domino Effect Argument | This is a defensive version of slippery-slope reasoning. It warns that one first step may trigger a sequence of later consequences. the crucial distinction is between “might develop” and “will develop”; the former can become a scare tactic if used to shut down discussion. | Possible-chain inflation. **Form:** If A is allowed, B might happen; B might lead to C; C might lead to disaster D. Therefore A must be rejected. | “If terminally ill patients can refuse aggressive treatment, this will lead to killing the disabled, then eugenics, then genocide.” The argument needs evidence for each transition. | Domino concerns can be serious in law and policy. They require plausible mechanisms, historical precedents, institutional incentives, and safeguards analysis. |
| M-15 `Sorites Fallacy` / Heap Argument / Bald Man Argument | Sorites reasoning exploits vague predicates by applying tiny changes repeatedly. Each small step seems acceptable, but the repeated sequence produces an absurd conclusion. This treats sorites as a species of slippery-slope reasoning connected to vagueness. | Repeated-small-step error. **Form:** Base case B0 is accepted. If Bn is accepted, Bn+1 differs only slightly and must also be accepted. Eventually absurd Bk follows. | “One grain is not a heap. Adding one grain cannot make a heap. Therefore no number of grains can make a heap.” | Sorites problems reveal real vagueness. They become fallacious in practical argument when vagueness is used to deny usable thresholds or policies. |
| M-16 `Argument to Moderation` / Middle Ground Fallacy / Golden Mean Fallacy | This fallacy assumes that the truth or best policy lies between two opposed positions. The middle may be reasonable, but it is not automatically correct because it is middle. | Middle-as-truth error. **Form:** A says X. B says not-X. Therefore the truth is halfway between X and not-X. | One person says a vaccine is safe; another says it contains tracking devices. “The truth is probably in the middle: it is somewhat dangerous and somewhat a tracking tool.” The midpoint is not evidence. | Compromise can be politically or practically useful. It becomes fallacious when compromise is treated as truth-finding. |
| M-17 `Nirvana Fallacy` / Perfect Solution Fallacy | This fallacy rejects a real but imperfect solution because it does not meet an ideal standard. The comparison is between actual policy and perfect fantasy, not between realistic alternatives. | Real-vs-perfect comparison error. **Form:** Proposal X does not solve all problems perfectly. Therefore X should be rejected. | “This harm-reduction policy will not eliminate drug abuse entirely, so it is useless.” The relevant question is whether it reduces harm compared with alternatives. | Some flaws are serious enough to reject a proposal. The fallacy lies in using perfection as the benchmark when the real choice is among imperfect options. |
| M-18 Appeal to the Moon / Impossible Standard | This fallacy demands an impossible, unrealistic, or wildly disproportionate standard before accepting a claim or policy. It makes rejection easy by setting the comparison point beyond reach. | Impossible-comparator error. **Form:** X is acceptable only if it meets impossible standard S. X fails S. Therefore reject X. | “Your traffic policy is worthless unless it prevents every accident in the city.” No policy can meet that standard. | High standards are appropriate in high-stakes settings. But the standard must be relevant, possible, and applied consistently. |
| M-19 Extreme Comparison Variant | This is the comparison variant of appeal to extremes. A moderate proposal is compared with an absurd endpoint, and the comparison is used to make the original proposal look equivalent to that endpoint. | Extreme-comparison distortion. **Form:** X is compared with extreme Y. Y is absurd. Therefore X is rejected. | “You support regulating political donations, so you must want the state to control every private political conversation.” The comparison manufactures the extreme. | The relevance version is listed under R-10. Here the focus is the distorted comparison. |
| M-20 `Historian’s Fallacy` | This fallacy judges past decisions as if the people at the time knew what later happened. It compares their choices to outcomes available only in hindsight. | Hindsight-comparison error. **Form:** Later outcome O is known now. Therefore historical actor should have acted as if O was known then. | “The company should obviously have invested everything in smartphones in 2005; the future was clear.” It was not clear to actors facing uncertainty at the time. | Historical judgment can criticize negligence if evidence was available then. The fallacy appears when later knowledge is silently imported into past decision contexts. |
| M-21 `Hypothesis Contrary to Fact` | This is the comparison and thought-experiment version of counterfactual overreach. A speculative alternative world is compared with the actual world as if the alternative path were already known. | Counterfactual-certainty error. **Form:** If unreal condition X had occurred, Y certainly would have happened. Therefore strong conclusion Z. | “If that reform had not passed, the country would definitely be prosperous now.” Many other variables could have changed. | The premise-framing variant is listed under P-22. Here the focus is counterfactual comparison. |
| M-22 Thought-Experiment Misuse | This fallacy treats an imagined case as if it directly settled a real-world issue. A thought experiment can clarify principles, but it usually simplifies away background facts, institutions, probabilities, psychology, and implementation costs. | Imagined-case overtransfer. **Form:** In hypothetical case H, judgment J seems right. Therefore in real case R, J must apply. | “In a trolley problem, pushing one person to save five seems permissible; therefore the state may sacrifice a minority group for economic growth.” The real case contains rights, institutions, abuse risks, uncertainty, and consent issues absent from the thought experiment. | Thought experiments are valuable for testing intuitions and principles. They become fallacious when their simplified structure is transferred directly to complex reality. |
| M-23 `False Model` / Map-Territory Confusion | This fallacy treats a model, map, simulation, game, or simplified scenario as if it were the reality it represents. It resembles analogy misuse but focuses on representational compression. | Model-reality transfer error. **Form:** Model M has property P. Therefore reality R has P in the same way. | “Our market simulation shows perfect rational choice, so real consumers will behave rationally.” The simulation may omit habit, fear, misinformation, and social influence. *Case from the outline: A complex technical subject is declared to be “just an old yin-yang principle,” as if a broad cultural model could replace the details of architecture, mechanism, and implementation.* | Models are useful because they simplify. The fallacy appears when simplification is forgotten and model results are treated as reality itself. |
| M-24 Moral Equivalence Fallacy | This fallacy treats two actions or systems as morally equivalent because they share a morally relevant feature, while ignoring differences in intention, scale, coercion, harm, accountability, or context. | Superficial moral comparison. **Form:** A and B both involve feature F. Therefore A and B are morally equivalent. | “A student copied one paragraph; a corporation fabricated safety data for years. Both lied, so they are morally the same.” The shared category does not erase differences in scale and consequence. | Moral comparison is legitimate when relevant moral dimensions are explicitly weighed. The fallacy lies in flattening morally important differences. |

## Fact, Value, and Norm Errors

These fallacies belong together because they confuse **what is true**, **what is good**, and **what ought to be done**. Some move too quickly from fact to value: “this is natural, traditional, legal, normal, popular, or convenient; therefore it is right.” Others reverse the direction and let moral desire decide empirical reality. The common error is allowing one kind of claim to do the work of another without the needed bridge principle.

| Fallacy                                                             | Explanation                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Mechanism                                                                                                                                | Example                                                                                                                                                                                              | Boundary                                                                                                                                                                               |
| --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| N-01 `Appeal to Nature` / Argumentum ad Naturam | This fallacy treats what is natural as automatically good, right, healthy, beautiful, or superior, and what is unnatural as automatically bad, wrong, harmful, or inferior. It should be distinguished from the naturalistic fallacy: appeal to nature specifically relies on the words “natural” or “unnatural,” and may concern non-moral evaluations as well. | Naturalness-as-value error. **Form:** X is natural. Therefore X is good/right/better. Or: X is unnatural. Therefore X is bad/wrong/worse. | “This herb is natural, so it must be safer than synthetic medicine.” Natural substances can be toxic; synthetic drugs can be safe when tested and dosed properly. | Naturalness can be relevant to ecology, compatibility, sustainability, or side effects. It becomes fallacious when naturalness alone is treated as proof of value or safety. |
| N-02 `Appeal to Tradition` / Argumentum ad Antiquitatem / Appeal to Antiquity / Appeal to Common Practice | This fallacy treats long-standing practice as evidence that the practice is correct. The formulation is direct: we have done X for generations; therefore we should keep doing X. Tradition may explain continuity, but it does not by itself justify the practice. | Past-practice-as-norm error. **Form:** We have always done X. Therefore we should continue X. | “Our family has always required sons to enter medicine and daughters to marry early, so that must remain our rule.” The fact that a rule is inherited does not prove it is justified. *Case from the outline: New workers are told that unpaid overtime is acceptable because the industry has always worked that way. The age of the practice is used in place of a justification for the practice.* | Tradition can preserve accumulated wisdom and social meaning. It becomes fallacious when the fact of age replaces reasons about value, harm, function, or justice. |
| N-03 Appeal to Novelty / Argumentum ad Novitatem | This fallacy treats something as better because it is new, modern, innovative, disruptive, or future-oriented. It is the mirror image of appeal to tradition. | Newness-as-value error. **Form:** X is new. Therefore X is better, truer, or more progressive. | “This education platform uses AI, so it must be superior to traditional teaching.” New technology may help, but it still needs evidence of learning outcomes. | Novelty can matter when old methods fail or new evidence changes conditions. The fallacy lies in treating newness itself as superiority. |
| N-04 Appeal to Normality | This fallacy treats what is statistically common or socially normal as morally right, rational, healthy, or desirable. It confuses frequency with normativity. | Normal-as-good error. **Form:** X is normal/common. Therefore X is good/right/healthy. | “Most people lie sometimes; therefore lying is morally acceptable.” Frequency does not establish moral permission. *Case from the outline: A company delays reimbursements and says the delay is normal because many companies do the same. Frequency is being used as permission.* | Normality may matter in medical baselines, social expectations, or risk assessment. It does not by itself decide value. |
| N-05 `Appeal to the Law` / Legalistic Fallacy | This fallacy treats legality as morality, or illegality as immorality. Law can be evidence of institutional judgment, but it does not automatically settle moral evaluation. The warning is against simply assuming that laws are good and right, because laws may become archaic or fall behind social change. | Legality-as-morality error. **Form:** X is legal. Therefore X is morally right. Or: X is illegal. Therefore X is morally wrong. | “The company’s conduct was legal, so it was ethical.” The law may set minimum compliance, not full moral justification. | Some illegal acts are plainly immoral, and some legal rules embody serious moral reasoning. The fallacy is treating legal status as the whole argument. |
